Definition of Argument (Ḥujja) and Its Composite Nature
Author defines argument and justifies prior discussion of simples and composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two essential components of a ḥujja (premises and assent) in any argument and distinguish a genuine proof from a mere statement.

Core Idea
The definiens of ḥujja: a composite of statements yielding assent.

The passage begins the third section on arguments. Before defining argument, the author explains why earlier sections covered simples and composites: because argument is composite. Understanding the parts is prerequisite. Then he gives the definition. Let's break it down.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
The term being defined is 'ḥujja' (argument). Its genus is 'expression composed of statements' (qawl mu'allaf min aqwāl), meaning it is a composite of multiple statements, not a single statement.
2
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'from which assent is obtained absolutely' (yaḥṣul minhu al-taṣdīq muṭlaqan). This distinguishes it from mere statements or rhetorical expressions that do not yield assent. 'Absolutely' includes both certain and probable assent.
3
Test the definition with examples
Consider 'All intoxicants are forbidden, wine is an intoxicant, therefore wine is forbidden.' This is a composite of statements, and it yields assent (to the conclusion). Thus it is a ḥujja. A single statement like 'Wine is forbidden' does not qualify.
The definition applies to valid arguments that produce belief, whether certain or probable.
4
Recognize the epistemological component
The definition highlights that logic is concerned not just with form but with producing knowledge or belief. This connects to the purpose of logic in the Islamic tradition: to safeguard the mind from error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجملة الثالثة : في الحجج وما يتعلق بها | [١٠٤ ] [ النوع الأول : في صورة القياس ] ولما كانت الحجة هي المقصود الأعظم والمطلوب الأهم فيما نحن بصدده؛ وهو المنطق وإنها في نفسها مركبة. والعلم بالمركب لا يحصل إلا بعد العلم بما يتركب منه ؛ وذلك هو المركب أيضًا إما من المفردات ، وإما من المركبات نحو الحمليّات والشرطيات فقد لزمنا الابتداء ببيان المفردات وتركيب أصناف ما يتركب منها إذن حتى أتينا على جميع ذلك البيان المتبرأ عن النقصان. ثم الحجة عبارة عن قول مؤلف من أقوال يحصل منه التصديق مطلقا؛ وهو الذي يطلق على الظني وغيره وهي ثلاثة قياس، والاستقراء، والتمثيل. وذلك لأنا إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإما أن يكون أحدهما أعم من الآخر أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول فإما أن نستدل بالأعم على الأخص وهو القياس، أو بالأخص على الأعم وهو الاستقراء؛ وإن كان الثاني وهو الذي لا يكون أحدهما أعم من الآخر فهو التمثيل. وقد يعبر هذا بعبارة أخرى / كما يقال [١٤٨ظ] الاستدلال بالشيء على الشيء [1] إما أن يكون بالكلي على الجزئي وهو القياس، [ب] أو بالجزئي على الكلي وهو الاستقراء، [ت] أو بالجزئي على الجزئي وهو التمثيل. ولا يقال كيف هو والاستدلال بالكلي على الكلي خارج عن هذه القسمة؛ إذ هو داخل فيها لما أن أحدهما لا يخلو من أن يكون داخلا تحت الكلي الآخر، وذلك هو الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي أو بالجزئي على الكلي أو لا يكون، وذلك هو الاستدلال بالجزئي على الجزئي؛ إذ الوصف المشترك بينهما هو الكلي بالنسبة إلى كل واحد منهما وإلا فلا يمكن الاستدلال بأحدهما على الآخر. ثم لكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة أمور قريبة منه نحو الضمير والرأي والعلامة» وأمثالها؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد على سبيل التفصيل إن شاء الله تعالى.
The third section: On arguments and what pertains to them. The first type: On the form of syllogism. Since the argument is the greatest aim and most important sought-after in what we are engaged in—namely logic—and it is in itself composite, and knowledge of the composite is only obtained after knowledge of what it is composed of, and that is also either from simples or from composites such as categoricals and conditionals, it was necessary for us to begin with an exposition of simples and the composition of the categories they are composed of, until we have completed all that exposition free from deficiency. Then the argument is an expression composed of statements from which assent is obtained absolutely; and it is applied to the probable and other than it. It is of three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy. That is because when we argue from one thing to another, either one of them is more general than the other, or not. If the former, then either we argue from the more general to the more specific—that is syllogism; or from the more specific to the more general—that is induction. If the latter, i.e., neither is more general than the other, then it is analogy. This is also expressed in another way, as it is said: arguing from something to something is either from the universal to the particular—that is syllogism; or from the particular to the universal—that is induction; or from the particular to the particular—that is analogy. And it is not said: how is it, for arguing from universal to universal is outside this division? For it is included in it, because one of them cannot but be subsumed under the other universal, and that is arguing from universal to particular or from particular to universal, or not—which is arguing from particular to particular; since the common description between them is universal relative to each one of them, otherwise it is not possible to argue from one to the other. Then for each of these three there are things close to it, such as conscience, opinion, sign, and the like; that will be known later in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition. It opens the third section of the work, which deals with arguments (ḥujaj) and related matters, specifically the first type concerning the form of syllogism. The author first justifies why the earlier sections discussed simple terms and composite statements: because argument is composite, and one must first understand its components. He then defines argument (ḥujja) as 'a statement composed of statements from which assent is obtained absolutely,' noting that it applies to both certain and probable assent. He divides arguments into three types: syllogism (qiyās), induction (istiqrā'), and analogy (tamthīl). The division is based on the relation of generality between the evidence and the conclusion. If the evidence is more general (a‘amm) than the conclusion (akhass), it is syllogism; if the evidence is more specific, it is induction; if neither is more general, it is analogy. The author also presents an alternative formulation using the terms 'universal' (kullī) and 'particular' (juz’ī). He anticipates an objection: what about arguing from universal to universal? He explains that this is included because either one universal subsumes the other (making it universal-to-particular) or they share a common universal (making it particular-to-particular). He then mentions that each type has associated concepts like conscience (ḍamīr), opinion (ra’y), and sign (‘alāma), to be explained later. This passage establishes the foundational classification of arguments in Islamic logic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a ḥujja?
A) It is a single statement.
B) It is composite and yields assent.
C) It must be certain.
D) It must be about universals.

Key Takeaway
A ḥujja is a composite of statements that yields assent, covering both certain and probable reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following statement: 'The world is temporal, everything temporal has a creator, therefore the world has a creator.' Identify the component statements and the assent obtained. Is this a ḥujja? Explain.

Three Types of Arguments: Syllogism, Induction, Analogy
Author defines argument and divides it into three types based on generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given argument as syllogism, induction, or analogy by comparing the generality of its evidence and conclusion.

Core Idea
Arguments are classified by the generality of evidence relative to conclusion.

After defining argument, the author classifies it into three types based on the relation of generality. This classification is foundational in logic. We will learn to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify evidence and conclusion
In any argument, there is something we argue from (the evidence or premises) and something we argue to (the conclusion). For example, in 'All intoxicants are forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden', the evidence is 'All intoxicants are forbidden' and 'wine is an intoxicant', the conclusion is 'wine is forbidden'.
2
Compare generality of terms
Look at the key terms in evidence and conclusion. Determine which is more general (a‘amm) and which is more specific (akhass). In the example, 'intoxicants' is more general than 'wine'.
Generality is about extension: a term that applies to more individuals is more general.
3
Apply the classification rule
If the evidence is more general than the conclusion, it is syllogism (qiyās). If the conclusion is more general, it is induction (istiqrā'). If neither is more general (they are of equal generality or one is not a subset of the other), it is analogy (tamthīl).
The passage uses the term 'a‘amm' for more general and 'akhass' for more specific.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Take the argument: 'This wine is forbidden, and this beer is forbidden, therefore all intoxicants are forbidden.' Here the evidence is about specific drinks, conclusion is general. So it is induction. Another: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; beer also intoxicates; therefore beer is forbidden.' Here evidence and conclusion are both specific, so it is analogy.

The third section: On arguments and what pertains to them. The first type: On the form of syllogism. Since the argument is the greatest aim and most important sought-after in what we are engaged in—namely logic—and it is in itself composite, and knowledge of the composite is only obtained after knowledge of what it is composed of, and that is also either from simples or from composites such as categoricals and conditionals, it was necessary for us to begin with an exposition of simples and the composition of the categories they are composed of, until we have completed all that exposition free from deficiency. Then the argument is an expression composed of statements from which assent is obtained absolutely; and it is applied to the probable and other than it. It is of three types: syllogism, induction, and analogy. That is because when we argue from one thing to another, either one of them is more general than the other, or not. If the former, then either we argue from the more general to the more specific—that is syllogism; or from the more specific to the more general—that is induction. If the latter, i.e., neither is more general than the other, then it is analogy. This is also expressed in another way, as it is said: arguing from something to something is either from the universal to the particular—that is syllogism; or from the particular to the universal—that is induction; or from the particular to the particular—that is analogy. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each argument: (a) All intoxicants are forbidden, wine is an intoxicant, so wine is forbidden. (b) This wine is forbidden, this beer is forbidden, so all intoxicants are forbidden. (c) Wine is forbidden due to intoxication, and beer shares intoxication, so beer is forbidden.

The Universal-Particular Formulation and Subsumption of Universal-to-Universal
Author refutes objection about universal-to-universal arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why arguing from a universal to another universal is not a fourth type and how it falls under syllogism or analogy.

Core Idea
Universal-to-universal arguments are subsumed, not a separate type.

The author presents an alternative formulation using universals and particulars. He anticipates an objection: what about universal-to-universal? He resolves it by showing it is covered. Let's analyze his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative formulation
The author restates the classification in terms of universals (kullī) and particulars (juz’ī): universal-to-particular is syllogism, particular-to-universal is induction, particular-to-particular is analogy.
2
Identify the potential gap
One might ask: what about arguing from a universal statement to another universal statement? For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every alcoholic beverage is an intoxicant; therefore every alcoholic beverage is forbidden.' Here both premises and conclusion are universal.
This seems to be a fourth pattern not covered by the three types.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author says it is included. In such an argument, one universal (e.g., 'intoxicant') is more general and subsumes the other universal (e.g., 'alcoholic beverage'). Therefore it is actually universal-to-particular (syllogism). If two universals are not in a subsumption relation, they must share a common universal, making it particular-to-particular (analogy).
The key is that without subsumption or a common universal, no argument is possible.
4
Apply the subsumption test
Given a universal-to-universal argument, check if one universal is a subset of the other. If yes, it reduces to syllogism. If not, they share a common attribute that is universal relative to each, reducing to analogy.
This ensures the three-type division is exhaustive.

The third section: On arguments and what pertains to them. The first type: On the form of syllogism. Since the argument is the greatest aim and most important sought-after in what we are engaged in—namely logic—and it is in itself composite, and knowledge of the composite is only obtained after knowledge of what it is composed of, and that is also either from simples or from composites such as categoricals and conditionals, it was necessary for us to begin with an exposition of simples and the composition of the categories they are composed of, until we have completed all that exposition free from deficiency. The author argues that reasoning from a universal to another universal is not a separate type because...
A) It is impossible.
B) It can be reduced to universal-to-particular or particular-to-particular.
C) It is the same as syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Every act that harms others is forbidden. Every form of backbiting harms others. Therefore every form of backbiting is forbidden.' Explain why this is not a fourth type but a syllogism, using the author's reasoning about subsumption.

Components of a Categorical Syllogism
Author explains why a middle term is necessary and defines the minor and major terms, premises, conjunction, and figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a categorical syllogism (minor term, major term, middle term, premises, figure) and explain the role of the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term is the logical bridge that connects the subject and predicate of a conclusion, making deduction possible.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that merely knowing the subject and predicate of a claim does not yield certainty; a third term—the middle term—is required to link them. We will dissect the structure of a categorical syllogism and learn the technical names for its parts. This foundational knowledge is essential for evaluating any deductive argument in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem the author solves
Recognize that the mind cannot judge a proposition like 'Body is originated' without a mediating link. The author argues that mere conception of the two terms is insufficient.
The problem is epistemological: how do we move from known terms to a new judgment?
2
Locate the middle term in the example
In the syllogism 'Every body is composite; every composite is originated; therefore every body is originated', find the term that appears in both premises. That is the middle term: 'composite'.
The middle term is repeated in both premises.
3
Map the roles of each term
Identify which term is the subject of the conclusion (minor term: 'body') and which is the predicate (major term: 'originated'). The middle term ('composite') links them.
The conclusion combines the minor and major terms directly.
4
Distinguish the premises
The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (صغرى); the premise containing the major term is the major premise (كبرى). Here, 'Every body is composite' is minor, 'Every composite is originated' is major.
The minor premise typically presents the middle term relating to the minor term.
5
Define the figure
The figure (شكل) is the arrangement of the middle term relative to the extremes. In this example, the middle term is the subject of the first premise and the predicate of the second – this is the first figure.
The figure determines the validity conditions of the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بحسب التركيب فإنه ينقسم إلى الأشكال الأربعة وذلك لأن مجرد تصور الطرفين أعني المحكوم عليه وبه في المسألة نحو الجسم والمحدث في قولنا الجسم محدث كما مر في المثال المذكور لما لم يكف في حكم الذهن بأنه محدث أم غير محدث؛ فلا بد من متوسط له نسبة معلومة إلى كلية المطلوب أو أجزائه نحو المؤلف في قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف وكل مؤلف محدث. فإن المؤلف له نسبة معلومة إلى الجسم وهو نسبة الصفة إلى الموصوف وإلى المحدث كذلك ؛ وهي نسبة الموصوف إلى الصفة ليكون العلم بتلك النسبة سببًا للعلم بالمطلوب المجهول. وذلك المتوسط لا بد وأن يكون متكرّرًا فيكون في القياس مقدمتان وثلاثة حدود. والمقدمتان تشتركان في حد من تلك الحدود وهو الحد الأوسط نحو المؤلف في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره، ولكل واحدة من المقدمتين حد مخصوص بتلك المقدمة نحو الجسم في المثال المذكور للأولى منهما والمحدث للثانية؛ والنتيجة تحصل من اجتماع هذين الحدين. فما هو موضوع في النتيجة يسمى حدا «أصغر»، وما هو محمول فيها يسمَّى حدا «أكبر». والمقدمة التي فيها الأصغر تسمى الصغرى، والتي فيها الأكبر تسمى «الكبرى». وتأليف المقدمتين يسمى «اقترانا وتأليفهما بالاقتران يسمَّى «قرينة» أيضًا. وما بحيث منه النتيجة لذاته يسمى قياسا ، وهيئة القياس من نسبة الأوسط إلى الطرفين تسمى «شكلا».
As for according to composition, it divides into four figures. That is because mere conception of the two terms—i.e., the subject and predicate in the issue, like 'body' and 'originated' in our statement 'Body is originated', as mentioned in the previous example—was not sufficient for the mind's judgment whether it is originated or not. So a middle term is necessary, having a known relation to the totality of the sought [conclusion] or its parts, like 'composite' in our statement 'Every body is composite and every composite is originated'. For 'composite' has a known relation to 'body'—the relation of attribute to described—and similarly to 'originated'—the relation of described to attribute—so that knowledge of that relation becomes the cause of knowledge of the unknown sought [conclusion]. That middle term must be repeated, so in the syllogism there are two premises and three terms. The two premises share a term among those terms—the middle term—like 'composite' in the example mentioned, and each premise has a term specific to that premise, like 'body' in the mentioned example for the first premise and 'originated' for the second. The conclusion results from the combination of these two terms. So what is the subject in the conclusion is called the 'minor term', and what is the predicate in it is called the 'major term'. The premise that contains the minor term is called the 'minor premise', and that which contains the major term is called the 'major premise'. The combination of the two premises is called 'conjunction' (iqtiran), and their composition by conjunction is also called 'qarina'. That from which the conclusion follows by itself is called a 'syllogism', and the form of the syllogism in terms of the relation of the middle term to the two extremes is called a 'figure' (shakl).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the fundamental components of a categorical syllogism. The author argues that merely considering the subject and predicate of a proposition (e.g., 'body' and 'originated' in 'Body is originated') does not enable the mind to judge the truth of the proposition; a middle term is needed to provide a known connection. The example given is the syllogism: 'Every body is composite; every composite is originated; therefore every body is originated.' The middle term 'composite' relates to 'body' as attribute to described, and to 'originated' as described to attribute. This middle term must appear in both premises, giving three terms and two premises. The author then defines the minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), minor premise (containing minor term), major premise (containing major term), conjunction (iqtiran/qarina) of premises, the syllogism itself, and finally the figure (shakl) as the arrangement of the middle term relative to the extremes.

Critique of Division as a Method for Syllogism
Author refutes those who think division is a syllogism or a method for acquiring syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical limitations of division (qisma) as a method for syllogism and distinguish when it yields a valid inference.

Core Idea
Division is not a syllogism; it only yields one when composed of disjunctive propositions.

Some logicians mistakenly considered division (qisma) to be a syllogism or a method for acquiring syllogisms. This passage critiques that view and clarifies that division is not a general logical tool but only produces a syllogism under specific conditions. We will analyze the author's argument and learn to assess when division is logically valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims being critiqued
The author reports two incorrect claims: (1) division is a syllogism for everything, and (2) division is a syllogism and demonstration for definitions. Recognize these as the targets of the critique.
2
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that if division were a demonstration for definitions, then definitions would require demonstration, leading to circularity. This is an epistemological objection.
3
Extract the author's positive thesis
The author states that division only yields a syllogism when composed of disjunctive propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-munfaṣila). This is the correct and limited scope of division.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider dividing acts into wajib, haram, etc. in usul al-fiqh. Does this classification alone constitute a syllogism? Only if the division is expressed as a disjunction (e.g., 'Every act is either wajib or not wajib') and a premise eliminates one option. Otherwise, it is mere taxonomy, not proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في القسمة ] منهم من ظن أن القسمة طريق من طرق اكتساب القياس بل هي القياس فمنهم من جعلها قياسًا على كل شيء، ومنهم من جعلها قياسا وبرهانا على الحد. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إلا وأن يكون الحد محتاجا إلى البرهان؛ فغيرهم يكون محتاجا إليه، وذلك هو القسمة والحق أن القسمة إنما يكون منها القياس المسوق إلى إنتاج ما يكون مؤلفًا من القضايا المنفصلة كما مر من قبل.
Chapter on Division. Among them are those who thought that division is a method of acquiring syllogism, rather it is the syllogism. Some made it a syllogism for everything, and some made it a syllogism and demonstration of the definition. That cannot be the case unless the definition is in need of demonstration; then others are in need of it, and that is division. The truth is that division only produces the syllogism that yields a composition of disjunctive propositions, as has passed before.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the view that division (qisma) is a valid method for acquiring syllogisms or is itself a syllogism. The author reports that some logicians considered division to be a syllogism for everything, while others considered it a syllogism and demonstration (burhān) for definitions. He argues that this would only be plausible if definitions themselves required demonstration, but then the definition would depend on what it is supposed to define. The correct view, according to the author, is that division only yields a syllogism when it is composed of disjunctive propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-munfaṣila), which he says was mentioned earlier. This is a typical critique of the Platonic method of division as a logical tool.

Division Cannot Establish Definitions
Author critiques division for constructing definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why logical division fails to produce a complete definition (hadd) and explain the specific shortcomings: inability to prove co-extensiveness and failure to distinguish essential from accidental attributes.

Core Idea
Division lists parts but can never guarantee the whole definition.

The author argues that while division can help enumerate differentiate, it cannot establish that a set of attributes is co-extensive with the definiendum. Moreover, division does not discriminate between essential and accidental features, which is crucial for a proper definition. This passage challenges the assumption that dividing a genus into species and collecting differentiate yields a valid definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method under critique
The author critiques the use of logical division (qisma) to construct a definition. Division starts with a genus, divides it into species by differentiate, and then combines them. Recognize that the target is the claim that this process can produce a hadd.
2
Understand the requirement of co-extensiveness (musawa)
A valid definition must apply to exactly the same class as the definiendum. The author states that division does not indicate co-extensiveness. Reflect on why a definition must be co-extensive: it must include all instances and exclude all non-instances.
Co-extensiveness is a key criterion for a proper hadd.
3
See why division fails to prove musawa
Even if you combine genus and differentiate step by step, division cannot show that the resulting combination is not too broad or too narrow. For example, 'rational mortal animal' might accidentally include some non-humans or exclude some humans if the division is incomplete.
4
Note the distinction between essential and accidental
The author points out that division does not separate essential (dhati) from accidental ('aradi) attributes, which is necessary for a definition that captures essence. Division lists differentiate without regard to whether they are essential.
A proper definition must include only essential attributes.
5
Conclude the limited utility of division
The author concludes that division is of little use in the core of syllogistic demonstration, especially for definitions. It may be a heuristic but not a proof tool. Summarize the three failures: (1) cannot identify the correct genus, (2) cannot prove co-extensiveness, (3) cannot distinguish essential from accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسمة لإثبات الحد فأول ما فيه أن القسمة لا تفيدك أن ما أخذته هو المحمول الأعم الذي يجب أن يقسم تقسيمه مثلا كالحيوان في هذا الموضع بل يجب أن يكون ذلك موضوعًا ثم تقول مثلا إن الإنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان إما مائت وإما غير مائت؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا يحصل منه الحد بل يجب أن تقول إن كل إنسان حيوان مائت؛ ثم تقول إن كل حيوان مائت إما ناطق وإما غير ناطق. ثم تقول إن الإنسان ناطق فيحصل أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق مائت. فإذا فعلت ذلك فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن هذا حد؛ إذ القسمة لا تدل على المساواة. ثم الحد كما تعلمه مؤلف من جنس ومن فصول، والجنس لا يكتسب بالقسمة، والفصول تكتسب به القسمة لا الذي يكتسب بالقسمة والمساواة ليس يمكن أن تبين بالقسمة أنها قد حصلت وأيضًا فإن مايكون من الذاتيات والعرضيات فذلك لا يتبين بالقسمة. فالقسمة إذن قليلة الجدوى في عمدة القياس وخصوصًا في الحد. ومع ذلك فإنها لا تخلو
And as for division for establishing the definition, the first thing about it is that division does not benefit you that what you have taken is the most general predicate which must be divided into its divisions, like 'animal' in this place. Rather, that must be a subject, then you say, for example, 'Man is an animal, and every animal is either mortal or immortal'; and it is among that from which the definition is not obtained. Rather, you must say, 'Every man is an animal that is mortal'; then you say, 'Every mortal animal is either rational or non-rational'; then you say, 'Man is rational'; so it results that man is a rational, mortal animal. If you do that, you cannot say that this is a definition, because division does not indicate co-extensiveness. Then the definition, as you know, is composed of a genus and differentiate, and the genus is not acquired by division, and the differentiate are acquired by division—rather, that which is acquired by division and co-extensiveness cannot be shown by division to have occurred. Also, what is from essential and accidental attributes is not shown by division. So division is of little use in the mainstay of syllogism, especially in definition. Nevertheless, it is not free...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a critique of using logical division (qisma) as a method for arriving at definitions (hadd). The author begins by pointing out a fundamental problem: division does not tell you which predicate is the most general (al-a'amm) that should be divided. For example, if you want to define man, you might start with 'animal' as the genus, but division alone does not establish that 'animal' is the correct genus—it must be taken as a subject. Then you proceed: 'Man is an animal; every animal is either mortal or immortal' — but this does not give you the definition. Instead, you must combine the genus with differentiate step by step: 'Every man is a mortal animal'; then 'Every mortal animal is rational or non-rational'; then 'Man is rational' — resulting in 'man is a rational, mortal animal'. Even then, this composite is not necessarily a definition because division does not prove co-extensiveness (musawa), i.e., that this combination applies to all and only men. A definition consists of a genus and differentiate; the genus cannot be acquired by division (it must be presupposed), and while differentiate can be listed via division, division cannot demonstrate that the resulting set is co-extensive. Furthermore, division cannot distinguish essential (dhati) from accidental ('aradi) attributes, which is necessary for a proper definition. The author concludes that division is of little benefit in the core of syllogistic demonstration, especially for definitions.

Analyzing Compound Terms and Particles in Syllogistic Analysis
Author advises how to handle compound limits and particles to avoid multiple analyses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between particles that are integral to the predicate and those that are merely relational, and rephrase propositions accordingly.

Core Idea
Not every preposition in a proposition is part of the predicate; some are just connectors.

In logical analysis, terms may be compound, and particles like 'في' and 'لام' can confuse analysis. The author provides criteria to decide when a particle is part of the predicate and when it is merely a relational link. This skill is essential for accurate syllogistic reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify compound terms
Look for terms that consist of multiple words, e.g., 'rational mortal animal'. Replace them with a single term if possible to simplify analysis.
If no single term exists, you may assign a placeholder or substitute a simpler expression.
2
Test the particle by conversion
For a proposition containing a particle, convert the proposition (change the order) and observe whether the particle can be omitted without changing the meaning.
Example: 'Good exists for wisdom' (الخير موجود للحكمة) converted to 'For wisdom is good' (للحكمة خير) drops the 'lam'.
3
Determine if particle is part of predicate
If conversion eliminates the particle and the meaning remains, the particle is merely relational. If the particle is essential for meaning, it is part of the predicate.
In 'A single knowledge exists in opposites', converting to 'Opposites have a single knowledge in them' retains the particle 'in', indicating it is integral.
4
Apply the test to your own examples
Take a proposition from fiqh or kalam, such as 'Wine has harm in it' (الخمر فيها ضرر). Convert it: 'Harm is in wine'? Does 'في' disappear? Test using the author's method.
This practice sharpens your ability to analyze predicate structure.
5
Generalize to both premises
The author notes that such particles can appear in both minor and major premises. When analyzing a syllogism, check each premise for particles and decide their status before proceeding.
This avoids mistaken term identification leading to invalid syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الوجوه من الاعتبارات يجب أن تراعيها حتى لا يتعدد التحليل ] ولنورد الآن وجوها من الاعتبارات يجب أن تراعى حتى لا يتعدد التحليل فتلك الاعتبارات في نفس الحدود وفي نفس الحكم لا بالقياس إلى النتيجة ولا أيضًا من جهة الدواخل، فمن ذلك ما يكون في نفس الحدود؛ وذلك لأن الحدود ربَّما لم تكن ألفاظا مفردة بل مركبة مثل الحيوان الناطق المائت فيتوسوس عليك إيراد الحدود، ويضعف تمييز بعضها عن البعض فاجتهد حتى تجد لجملة جملة منها اسما مفردًا. فإن لم تجد فلا عليك أن تضع لجملة فيها اسما مفردًا أو ربما تكون الأولى أن يستبدل اسما مكان اسم، وأن تصلح خللا مقولاً في العبارة، وأن تتأمل حال الألفاظ التي هي أدوات أو كالأدوات، فهي حقها أن يكون جزءا من جملة المحمول فيعرض من تأملها ما عرض فيما سلف مثل قولك إن علمًا واحدًا موجود في الأضداد، وإن الخير موجود للحكمة؛ فإن حرف «في» جزء من المحمول؛ لأن معناه الأضداد فيها علم واحد، وحرف ل) في الأجزاء ليس جزءًا من شيء بل هو حرف دال على الربط. فإذا وجدتَ شيئًا من هذا الجنس فانظر هل هو جزء؛ فاحتفظه جزءا، وأضفه إلى ما هو الأقرب واجعل منهما حدا واحدا، واطرح الآخر، واقلب القضية على عبارة لا يحوِّجك إلى استعمال ذلك. فإنك إذا قلت الخير موجود للحكمة احتجت إلى هذا الحرف وهو اللام، وإذا قلت للحكمة خير استغنيت عنه؛ تعلم أن حرف اللام ليس جزءًا من المحمول أصلا. فأما إذا قلتَ علم واحد في الأضداد ثمَّ قلت الأضداد فيها علم واحد فلك حاجة إلى لفظة «في» أو «ما» يقوم معا فيها. وقد سبق أن يكون مثل هذه اللفظة في الصغرى والكبرى جميعًا دالة على الجزء كما في قولك كل كيفية فيها تصديق،
Chapter: On aspects of considerations that must be observed to avoid multiple analysis. Let us now present aspects of considerations that must be observed to avoid multiple analysis: those considerations are in the limits themselves and in the judgment itself, not with respect to the conclusion nor from the perspective of inducers. Among them is what is in the limits themselves, because limits sometimes are not single words but compound, like 'rational mortal animal', so you become anxious about presenting the limits and your distinction of some from others weakens. So strive to find for each aggregate of them a single name. If you do not find, then you may assign to the aggregate a single name, or perhaps it is better to substitute one name for another, and to correct a defect in the expression, and to ponder the state of words that are tools or like tools: their right is to be part of the predicate complex, so contemplation of them brings about what occurred previously, like your saying 'a single knowledge exists in opposites', and 'good exists for wisdom'; for the particle 'in' is part of the predicate because its meaning is 'opposites have a single knowledge in them', and the particle 'l' in parts is not part of anything but is a particle indicating relation. So if you find something of this kind, look: is it a part? Then preserve it as a part, add it to what is nearest and make them a single limit, and discard the other, and convert the proposition to an expression that does not require you to use that. For if you say 'good exists for wisdom', you need this particle, which is the lam; if you say 'for wisdom is good', you dispense with it; you know that the lam is not part of the predicate at all. But if you say 'a single knowledge in opposites', then say 'opposites have a single knowledge in them', you need the word 'in' or 'what' stands with them. And it has preceded that such a word, in both the minor and major, can indicate a part, as in your saying 'every quality has in it affirmation'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a methodological section on handling compound limits and particles in logical analysis to avoid multiple interpretations. The author begins by stating the goal: considerations must be observed so that analysis does not become multiple (i.e., ambiguous or divided). These considerations relate to the limits (terms) themselves and the judgment, not to the conclusion or external inducers. He focuses first on the limits: sometimes limits are compound (e.g., 'rational mortal animal'), making it hard to distinguish parts. The remedy is to find a single term for each aggregate, if possible, or substitute a simpler expression. Next, he discusses particles (adawat) that function like tools, such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for). These may be part of the predicate or merely relational. He illustrates with the example: 'a single knowledge exists in opposites'—here 'في' is part of the predicate because the meaning is 'opposites have knowledge in them'. In contrast, 'good exists for wisdom'—the 'لام' is not part of the predicate; by converting to 'for wisdom is good', one omits the particle, showing it was only a relational link. The author advises: when you encounter such a particle, determine if it is part of the limit; if yes, incorporate it; if not, discard it and rephrase the proposition. He recalls a previous point that such particles can indicate a part in both premises, e.g., 'every quality has in it affirmation'. This is crucial in fiqh reasoning when analyzing legal propositions: e.g., 'wine has harm in it'—'في' may be part of the predicate or a mere link. A careful logician distinguishes these to avoid fallacious arguments.

الأدوات
جزء من المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'al-khayr mawjud lil-hikma' (good exists for wisdom), how does the author show that 'lam' is not part of the predicate?

The distinction between integral and relational particles is crucial for accurate term analysis in syllogisms, as demonstrated by the conversion test.

Parsing Composite Definitions and Prepositions in Predicates
Author instructs on handling composite definitions and prepositions in propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a preposition is part of the predicate versus a mere connector, and apply the method of reducing composite definitions to single terms.

Core Idea
Prepositions can be part of the predicate or mere connectors—learn to parse correctly to avoid logical errors.

This passage from the Mantiq Kabir teaches a crucial parsing skill: composite definitions like 'al-hayawan al-natiq al-ma'it' must be reduced to single words to avoid confusion, and prepositions like 'fi' and 'lam' play different roles in the predicate. Understanding this prevents multiple analyses and ensures precision in logical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify composite definitions
Look for phrases in definitions that are expressed in multiple words but function as a single concept, e.g., 'al-hayawan al-natiq al-ma'it' (the speaking dying animal).
Composite definitions can cause confusion in distinguishing parts of the definition.
2
Reduce composite definitions to single terms
Strive to find a single word that captures the entire meaning of the composite phrase. If none exists, assign a placeholder term or substitute a better word.
This simplifies analysis and prevents multiple interpretations.
3
Determine the role of prepositions in predicates
Examine each preposition in the predicate: if it is integral to the meaning (e.g., 'fi' in 'ilm mawjud fi al-addad'), it is part of the predicate; if it merely indicates a relation (e.g., 'lam' in 'al-khayr mawjud lil-hikma'), it is a connector.
The author contrasts 'fi' (part of predicate) with 'lam' (connector).
4
Test with additional examples
Practice identifying the role of prepositions in other propositions from Islamic sciences, such as 'al-ilm mawjud fi al-qalb' and 'al-salatu fardun 'ala kulli muslimin'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في الوجوه من الاعتبارات يجب أن تراعيها حتى لا يتعدد التحليل ] ولنورد الآن وجوها من الاعتبارات يجب أن تراعى حتى لا يتعدد التحليل فتلك الاعتبارات في نفس الحدود وفي نفس الحكم لا بالقياس إلى النتيجة ولا أيضًا من جهة الدواخل، فمن ذلك ما يكون في نفس الحدود؛ وذلك لأن الحدود ربَّما لم تكن ألفاظا مفردة بل مركبة مثل الحيوان الناطق المائت فيتوسوس عليك إيراد الحدود، ويضعف تمييز بعضها عن البعض فاجتهد حتى تجد لجملة جملة منها اسما مفردًا. فإن لم تجد فلا عليك أن تضع لجملة فيها اسما مفردًا أو ربما تكون الأولى أن يستبدل اسما مكان اسم، وأن تصلح خللا مقولاً في العبارة، وأن تتأمل حال الألفاظ التي هي أدوات أو كالأدوات، فهي حقها أن يكون جزءا من جملة المحمول فيعرض من تأملها ما عرض فيما سلف مثل قولك إن علمًا واحدًا موجود في الأضداد، وإن الخير موجود للحكمة؛ فإن حرف «في» جزء من المحمول؛ لأن معناه الأضداد فيها علم واحد، وحرف اللام في الأجزاء ليس جزءًا من شيء بل هو حرف دال على الربط.
Chapter: On the aspects of considerations that you must observe so that analysis does not multiply. We will now present aspects of considerations that must be observed so that analysis does not multiply. Those considerations are in the definitions themselves and in the judgment itself, not in relation to the conclusion nor from the perspective of the internal aspects. Among them is what is in the definitions themselves: because definitions might not be single words but composite, such as 'the speaking dying animal' – so the introduction of definitions becomes problematic and distinguishing some from others becomes weak. Therefore strive until you find for each phrase a single word. If you do not find, then it is fine to assign a single word to a phrase, or perhaps it is better to substitute one word for another, to fix a reported defect in the expression, and to examine the state of words that are tools or like tools. Their right is to be part of the predicate sentence; then examination of them presents what was presented earlier, such as your saying 'a single knowledge exists in opposites', and 'good exists for wisdom'. For the letter 'in' is part of the predicate because its meaning is 'opposites have a single knowledge', and the letter 'lam' in parts is not part of anything but is a letter indicating connection.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a methodological instruction on how to handle definitions and propositions to avoid multiple analyses. The author begins by stating that the considerations are in the definitions themselves and in the judgment itself, not in relation to the conclusion or internal aspects. Then he focuses on definitions: they may be composite like 'the speaking dying animal' (al-hayawan al-natiq al-ma'it), which makes it difficult to distinguish parts. The advice is to strive to find a single word for each phrase. If impossible, one may assign a single word or substitute a word to fix defects. Next, he turns to prepositions (أدوات). These may be part of the predicate sentence. For example, in 'a single knowledge exists in opposites', the word 'in' (في) is part of the predicate because the meaning is 'opposites have a single knowledge'. In contrast, in 'good exists for wisdom', the letter 'lam' is not part of anything but a connector indicating relation. Thus, the student must carefully analyze whether a preposition is integral to the predicate or merely relational.

The Rule of Repeated Terms in Syllogisms
Author explains rule for repeated terms in definitions and syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly assign repeated terms to the predicate in categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
A repeated term always belongs to the predicate, never the subject.

In logical statements, a term may appear multiple times. A common error is to think the repetition is part of the subject. The author demonstrates with the example 'Justice is good' that the repeated term functions as part of the predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repeated term
Find the term that occurs more than once in the syllogistic sequence.
2
Analyze the first occurrence
In the first premise, determine the role of that term. For 'Justice is good', 'good' is the predicate.
3
Analyze the second occurrence
In the second premise 'every good is known to be good', 'good' is the subject (middle term), but note that the predicate contains 'good' again.
4
Apply the rule
The repetition of 'good' in the conclusion's predicate is not part of the subject; it is part of the predicate. Recognize that when a term is repeated 'from a certain aspect', that aspect qualifies the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه قد يعرض لبعض الحدود أن يوجد مكرّرًا فيظن أنه من حيث هو مكرر؛ ولا يكون كذلك كما في قولنا العدل خير، وكل خير فإنه يعلم أنه خير فالعدل يعلم أنه خير فالخير هنا يكون مرة حدا أوسط، ومرة جزءًا من الأكبر فيجب أن تعلم أنه حيث ما قيل من جهة كذا فإنه في المحمول دون الموضوع. فإن قولك «ج» كذا من جهة ما هو «ج» ليس (ج) الثانية جزءًا من الموضوع بل المحمول. ولهذا وقع الغلط في قول من يقول: «إن الموجود من جهة ما موجود إما قائم بنفسه وإما غير قائم بنفسه». ولو كان الموجود من جهة ما هو موجود غير قائم بنفسه لوجب أن يكون كل موجود موصوفا أنه قائم بنفسه. وكذلك إن كان من تلك الجهة قائما بنفسه وجب أن يكون كل موجود قائما بنفسه : وهذا خلف. ولا يعلم أن المقابل للموجبة منهما هو أنه ليس من جهة ما هو موجود قائما بنفسه فيدخل السلب على القائم بنفسه مقرونا به من جهة ما هو موجود. ويكون من جهة ما هو موجود داخلا في المحمول فلا يلزم من ذلك المحال، فجميع أمثال هذه يجب أن يوجد المكرر منها في جملة المحمول. فإن كان في الأوسط في مثل الضرب المشار إليه وقع أيضًا في الأكبر. وإن لم يجر الأمر على هذه الجهة لم يكن للكلام معنى، ولم ينحل إلى الضروب ومن الأمثلة لهذا الباب الإنسان محسوس، وكل محسوس فإنه يعدم من جهة ما هو محسوس؛ وكلما أردت أن ينتج في أمثال هذه الأشياء بشرط فيجب عليك أن تأخذ الأكبر في الأوسط بالشرط ثمَّ تحلل. وإن صح إنتاجك مرسلا فخذ ذلك مرسلا ثم حلل مثال الأول إن أردت أن ينتج أن الخير معلوم ما؛ فقل لأنه موجود ما.
Know that sometimes a definition is found with a repeated term, and one thinks it is repeated as such, but it is not, as in our saying: 'Justice is good, and every good is known to be good, so justice is known to be good.' Here 'good' is once a middle term and once a part of the major. So you must know that wherever it is said 'from a certain aspect', it is in the predicate, not the subject. For your saying 'J is such from the aspect that it is J' — the second J is not part of the subject but of the predicate. Hence the mistake occurred in the saying: 'The existent, from the aspect that it is existent, is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent.' If the existent from the aspect that it is existent were not self-subsistent, it would be necessary that every existent be described as self-subsistent. Likewise, if from that aspect it were self-subsistent, it would be necessary that every existent be self-subsistent — which is absurd. One does not realize that the contradictory of the affirmative of these two is that it is not, from the aspect that it is existent, self-subsistent, so the negation enters upon 'self-subsistent' conjoined with it from the aspect that it is existent. And 'from the aspect that it is existent' enters into the predicate, so that impossibility does not follow. So in all such cases, the repeated term must occur in the whole of the predicate. If it is in the middle term in such a form, it also occurs in the major. If the matter is not conducted in this way, the statement has no meaning and cannot be resolved into the figures. Among the examples of this topic: 'Man is sensible, and every sensible thing ceases to exist from the aspect that it is sensible.' Whenever you want to produce a conclusion in such things with a condition, you must take the major into the middle with the condition, then analyze. If your production is unconditionally correct, take it unconditionally and then analyze. An example of the first: if you want to produce that 'the good is something known', say: 'because it is something existent.'
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle logical issue in definitions and syllogisms: the occurrence of a repeated term that appears to be part of the subject but is actually part of the predicate. The author begins by illustrating with the example 'Justice is good; every good is known to be good; therefore justice is known to be good.' Here 'good' appears twice: once as the middle term and once as part of the major term 'known to be good'. The key point is that when a term is repeated 'from a certain aspect' (min haythu ma huwa), the repetition belongs to the predicate, not the subject. For instance, saying 'J is such from the aspect that it is J' means the second 'J' is in the predicate. The author then critiques a mistaken statement: 'The existent, from the aspect that it is existent, is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent.' This leads to absurdity because if 'existent from that aspect' were not self-subsistent, then every existent would be not self-subsistent, which is false; similarly if it were self-subsistent. The mistake is that the negation should be applied to 'self-subsistent' while keeping the aspect qualifier within the predicate, thus avoiding the universal claim. The correct analysis is that the repeated term ('from the aspect that it is existent') is part of the predicate, not the subject. The author generalizes: in all such cases, the repeated term must be in the predicate. If it appears in the middle term, it also appears in the major. Without this treatment, the argument cannot be resolved into syllogistic figures. The author gives another example: 'Man is sensible, and every sensible thing ceases to exist from the aspect that it is sensible.' For sound syllogistic reasoning, one must incorporate the condition (the aspect) into the middle term when taking the major premise. The final example: to prove 'the good is something known', one can say 'because it is something existent' — using the unconditional form. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes precision in logical analysis to avoid fallacious reasoning.

Key Terms
حد
مكرر
حد أوسط
أكبر
محمول

Key Takeaway
When a term is repeated in a syllogism, it typically belongs to the predicate, not the subject.

The Fallacy of Misplacing the Aspectual Qualifier
Author refutes a mistake about existential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct the fallacy of treating an aspectual qualifier as part of the subject rather than the predicate.

Core Idea
Qualifying a term 'from a certain aspect' affects the predicate, not the subject.

A common logical mistake is to use 'al-mawjud min haythu ma huwa mawjud' (the existent from the aspect that it is existent) in a way that implies universal claims about existence. The author shows how to properly analyze such statements to avoid contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacious argument
The statement 'The existent, from the aspect that it is existent, is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent' leads to a paradox.
2
Understand the paradox
If the aspectual phrase is attached to the subject, it forces a universal claim about all existents: either all are self-subsistent or none are, both absurd.
3
Locate the error
The aspectual phrase should be attached to the predicate, not the subject. The correct reading is: 'The existent is, from the aspect that it is existent, either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent.'
4
Apply the correction
Place the aspectual qualifier inside the predicate, so that the negation applies only to 'self-subsistent' while the qualifier remains. This avoids the universal claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition and Four Terms of Tamthil
Author defines tamthil and enumerates its four terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tamthil and identify its four logical terms (major, middle, minor, similar) in any analogical argument.

Core Idea
Tamthil transfers a ruling from original to branch via a common cause, structured by four terms.

Tamthil is the logical engine behind juristic analogy (qiyas). In this lesson, we unpack its definition and the four terms that govern its structure. Understanding these terms will allow you to analyze any argument by analogy in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Find the author's definition of tamthil: 'a judgment upon a particular due to its existence in another particular due to similarity' and the best definition 'transference of ruling from original to branch by a unified cause'.
2
Identify the four terms
The text lists four terms: major (al-akbar), middle (al-awsat), minor (al-asghar), and similar (shabih). Note that the middle is predicated on both the minor and its similar.
3
Map the example
Consider the example: 'this building is originated because it is a body, so likewise the sky is originated for being a body.' Identify the building as minor, sky as similar, bodily nature as middle, and originated as major.
4
Understand the role of the middle
The middle is the common cause ('illa) that necessitates the ruling in both the original and the branch. Without it, the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في التمثيل ] وأما التمثيل فهو الحكم على جزئي لوجوده في جزئي آخر؛ وهو متفق عليه لمشابهة بينهما كما مرَّ في بيان الأقيسة المتفقهة. فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة عند المتفقه (۱) ، أحسنها أن يقال تعدية الحكم المتحد من الأصل إلى الفرع بعلَّة متحدة فيهما. فيقال مثلا هذا البناء حادث لأنه جسم فكذا السماء لكونها جسما. وإنه أي التمثيل مشتمل على أربعة حدود: [أ] الأكبر وهو كلي [ب] والأوسط وهو كلي أيضًا وإنه محمول على [ت] الأصغر وعلى [ث] شبيهه فيكون الأصغر حدا، وشبيهه كذلك. فالحكم في الأصل [٢٦٩] وهو الصورة المتفق عليها يضاف إلى ما هو المشترك بينه وبين الفرع وهو الصورة المختلف فيها ؛ وإنه لا يضاف إليه إلا وأن يكون علة لوجوده، فيكون علة. وهذه العلة متحققة في الفرع فيثبت الحكم في الفرع لكن لا يكون حكمًا قطعيا لاحتمال أن يكون مضافًا في تلك الصورة إلى ما يكون مختصا بتلك الصورة، وأن يكون المانع متحققا في هذه الصورة أيضًا حتى إذا كان المشترك علة فلا يلزم من وجود تلك العلة في هذه الصورة الحكم فيها. والعلة [1] قد تكون مطردة ومنعكسة حتى يلزم من وجودها وجود الحكم، ومن عدمها عدم الحكم كذلك. [ب] وقد تكون مطردة غير منعكسة، ولكل واحدة منهما نظائر في المعقولات والمحسوسات فيقال مثلا الحكم متحقق في تلك الصورة لوجود هذه العلة، وهذه العلة متحققة في هذه الصورة، فيكون الحكم متحققا فيها . فكأنه أي التمثيل مركب من الاستقراء والقياس لما أنه يتبدل بالحكم في محل الوفاق على ارتباطه بالوصف المشترك. ثمَّ به على ثبوته في محل آخر فأوله الاستدلال بالخاص على العام، وآخره الاستدلال بالعام على الخاص. وقد يعبر بعبارة أخرى فيقال كلَّما كان البناء حادثا كانت الجسمية علة للحدوث ، وكلَّما كانت الجسمية علة للحدوث كانت السماء حادة لاشتمالها على العلة وامتناع تخلف المعلول عن العلة فينتج كلَّما كان البناء حادثا كانت السماء حادة. ثم يقال لكن البناء حادث (۱) فالسماء إذن حادة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون مركبا من قياس اقتراني أو قياس استثنائي.
Chapter on al-Tamthil. As for al-tamthil, it is a judgment upon a particular due to its existence in another particular; and it is agreed upon due to a similarity between them as has passed in the discussion of agreed-upon syllogisms. For it is known by different definitions among the jurists, the best of which is to say: the transference of the shared ruling from the original to the branch by a unified cause present in both. So it is said, for example, this building is originated because it is a body, and likewise the sky for being a body. And it (i.e., tamthil) comprises four terms: [a] the major, which is universal; [b] the middle, which is also universal, and it is predicated on [t] the minor and on [th] its similar; so the minor is a term, and its similar likewise. The ruling in the original, which is the agreed-upon form, is attributed to what is common between it and the branch, which is the differing form; and it is not attributed to it unless it is a cause for its existence, so it becomes a cause. And this cause is realized in the branch, so the ruling is established in the branch, but it is not a certain judgment due to the possibility that it might be attributed in that form to what is specific to that form, and that the obstacle might be realized in this form as well such that even if the commonality is a cause, the existence of that cause in this form does not necessitate the ruling in it. And the cause may be [a] constant and reciprocal such that its existence necessitates the existence of the ruling, and its absence necessitates the absence of the ruling likewise. [b] And it may be constant but not reciprocal; and each of them has parallels in the intelligible and sensible realms. So it is said, for example, the ruling is realized in that form due to the existence of this cause, and this cause is realized in this form, so the ruling is realized in it. So it is as if tamthil is composed of induction and syllogism because it begins with the ruling in the locus of agreement regarding its connection to the shared attribute, then proceeds to its establishment in another locus; so its beginning is inference from the particular to the universal, and its end is inference from the universal to the particular. And it may be expressed by another phrase: it is said, whenever the building is originated, bodily nature is the cause of origination; and whenever bodily nature is the cause of origination, the sky is originated because it includes the cause and the impossibility of the effect lagging behind the cause; so it results that whenever the building is originated, the sky is originated. Then it is said: but the building is originated, so the sky is therefore originated; and then it follows that it is composed of a categorical syllogism or a conditional syllogism.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage defines tamthil (analogical reasoning) as understood in Islamic logic, particularly as used by jurists. The author begins by stating that tamthil is a judgment on one particular based on its existence in another particular, due to similarity. He notes that among various definitions, the best is 'transferring the shared ruling from the original (asl) to the branch (far') by a unified cause ('illa) present in both.' An example is given: 'This building is originated (hadith) because it is a body, so likewise the sky is originated because it is a body.' The author then explains that tamthil consists of four terms: the major (al-akbar, the universal predicate in the conclusion), the middle (al-awsat, the common cause), the minor (al-asghar, the subject of the original), and the similar (shabih, the subject of the branch). The middle is predicated on both the minor and the similar. The ruling in the original is attributed to the common attribute (the cause), and that cause must be present in the branch for the ruling to transfer. However, the result is not certain because the cause might be specific to the original form, or there might be an obstacle (mani') in the branch. The author then distinguishes two types of cause: (a) constant and reciprocal (mutarida wa mun'akisa) where existence of cause entails ruling and absence entails absence, and (b) constant but not reciprocal (mutarida ghayr mun'akisa). Each has examples in intelligibles and sensibles. Next, he analyzes tamthil as composed of induction (istiqra') and syllogism (qiyas): it starts with a particular judgment (the original) and infers a universal relation (the common attribute), then applies that universal to another particular (the branch). He illustrates with a conditional expression: 'Whenever the building is originated, bodily nature is the cause of origination; and whenever bodily nature is the cause of origination, the sky is originated; therefore whenever the building is originated, the sky is originated.' Then by affirming the antecedent, the conclusion is reached. Thus tamthil can be reduced to either a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) or a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i).

مطردة ومنعكسة

According to the text, what are the four terms (حدود) of tamthil?
A) Major, middle, minor, and similar
B) Original, branch, cause, and effect
C) Universal, particular, subject, and predicate
D) Genus, species, differentia, and accident

Key Takeaway
Tamthil analogical reasoning is defined by four terms: major, middle, minor, and similar, where the middle (cause) links the original and branch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the fiqh example: 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication; khamr (grape wine) is intoxicating, so it is forbidden.' Identify the four terms of tamthil (major, middle, minor, similar).
?

6 min
Types of Cause in Tamthil
Author classifies causes into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a cause that is constant and reciprocal (mutarida mun'akisa) and one that is constant only, and explain their implications for certainty of the analogical conclusion.

Core Idea
A cause that is constant and reciprocal yields a certain conclusion; constant only yields probable.

Not all causes are equal. The author distinguishes two types of cause in tamthil: one that is both constant and reciprocal (existence entails ruling, absence entails absence), and one that is constant only. This distinction determines whether the analogical conclusion is certain or merely probable.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of constant and reciprocal
The text says: 'the cause may be constant and reciprocal such that its existence necessitates the existence of the ruling, and its absence necessitates the absence of the ruling.'
2
Contrast with constant only
The other type is constant but not reciprocal: the cause always gives the ruling, but its absence does not necessarily remove the ruling.
3
Consider examples
The author says each type has parallels in intelligibles and sensibles. For sensibles, fire and heat: fire causes heat, and without fire there is no heat (constant and reciprocal). For intelligibles, a legal cause might be constant but not reciprocal.
4
Understand why tamthil is probabilistic
Even if the cause is present in the branch, there may be a specific attribute in the original or an obstacle in the branch that prevents the ruling. Hence, tamthil rarely yields certainty.

Checkpoint
What does it mean for a cause to be مطردة ومنعكسة (constant and reciprocal)?
A) The cause always produces the ruling and the ruling always implies the cause
B) The cause is found in all cases but not always absent when the ruling is absent
C) The cause is clear and unambiguous
D) The cause is both necessary and sufficient for the ruling

Key Takeaway
A cause that is constant and reciprocal gives certainty; constant-only gives only probability, making tamthil typically non-demonstrative.

Tamthil: Induction from the Particular then Syllogism back to Particular
Author analyzes tamthil into induction and syllogism, with a conditional example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an analogical argument as a two-step process: induction (particular to universal) followed by syllogism (universal to particular).

Core Idea
Tamthil infers a universal cause from the original, then deduces the ruling for the branch.

The author reveals the logical structure of tamthil: it is not a simple inference but a compound of induction and syllogism. By breaking it down, we see how it moves from a specific case to a general rule and back to another specific case. This analysis also shows why the conclusion is not always certain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts
The text says tamthil is composed of induction (istiqra') and syllogism (qiyas). First, from the original particular, we infer a universal cause. Then, from that universal cause, we deduce the ruling for the branch.
2
Examine the conditional syllogism example
The author gives: 'Whenever the building is originated, bodily nature is the cause of origination; and whenever bodily nature is the cause of origination, the sky is originated; therefore whenever the building is originated, the sky is originated.' This is a conditional syllogism with transitive premises.
3
See the modus ponens step
Then the author adds: 'The building is originated, so the sky is originated.' This is affirming the antecedent, yielding the conclusion.
4
Recognize alternative formulation
The text notes it can be expressed as a categorical syllogism or a conditional syllogism. The key is that tamthil combines inductive generalization with deductive application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage defines tamthil (analogical reasoning) as understood in Islamic logic, particularly as used by jurists. The author begins by stating that tamthil is a judgment on one particular based on its existence in another particular, due to similarity. He notes that among various definitions, the best is 'transferring the shared ruling from the original (asl) to the branch (far') by a unified cause ('illa) present in both.' An example is given: 'This building is originated (hadith) because it is a body, so likewise the sky is originated because it is a body.' The author then explains that tamthil consists of four terms: the major (al-akbar, the universal predicate in the conclusion), the middle (al-awsat, the common cause), the minor (al-asghar, the subject of the original), and the similar (shabih, the subject of the branch). The middle is predicated on both the minor and the similar. The ruling in the original is attributed to the common attribute (the cause), and that cause must be present in the branch for the ruling to transfer. However, the result is not certain because the cause might be specific to the original form, or there might be an obstacle (mani') in the branch. The author then distinguishes two types of cause: (a) constant and reciprocal (mutarida wa mun'akisa) where existence of cause entails ruling and absence entails absence, and (b) constant but not reciprocal (mutarida ghayr mun'akisa). Each has examples in intelligibles and sensibles. Next, he analyzes tamthil as composed of induction (istiqra') and syllogism (qiyas): it starts with a particular judgment (the original) and infers a universal relation (the common attribute), then applies that universal to another particular (the branch). He illustrates with a conditional expression: 'Whenever the building is originated, bodily nature is the cause of origination; and whenever bodily nature is the cause of origination, the sky is originated; therefore whenever the building is originated, the sky is originated.' Then by affirming the antecedent, the conclusion is reached. Thus tamthil can be reduced to either a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) or a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i).

القياس
التمثيل

Key Takeaway
Tamthil is a compound argument: it begins with induction (particular to universal) and ends with a syllogism (universal to particular).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reconstruct the conditional syllogism form from the passage using your own example from usul al-fiqh or kalam.

Classification of Definitions (Hadd and Rasm)
Author classifies definitions and descriptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm) as perfect or imperfect by identifying the genus and differentia in any Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Definitions give essential distinction; descriptions give accidental distinction – knowing which is used is key to precise reasoning.

In Islamic logic, defining terms with precision is fundamental for rigorous discourse, whether in fiqh, usul, or kalam. The author divides definitions into four types based on whether they provide essential or accidental distinction and whether they are complete. This classification allows you to evaluate the quality of any definition you encounter in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Determine the type of distinction
Identify whether the definition aims to provide essential distinction (the 'what-it-is') or accidental distinction (a characteristic property). This is the first division in the passage: hadd (essential) vs rasm (accidental).
Essential distinction captures the essence; accidental captures non-essential properties.
2
Assess completeness
Check if the definition includes both genus and differentia (tamm/perfect) or lacks one (naqis/imperfect). The passage classifies each type as perfect or imperfect accordingly.
Perfect hadd: genus + differentia. Imperfect hadd: only one of them.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the definition of 'wine' from fiqh: 'an intoxicating beverage'. The genus is 'beverage', the differentia is 'intoxicating'. Since intoxication is the cause of prohibition, it is an essential property, making this a hadd tamm.
You can test other definitions, e.g., 'prayer' as 'a set of specific acts beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'.
4
Contrast with a description
Consider 'animal that laughs' for man. The genus is 'animal', the differentia 'laughs' is an accident. This is rasm tamm. Recognizing the difference between essential and accidental distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions in Islamic sciences.
In usul al-fiqh, the property of 'being male' for a witness is an accidental distinction.

وأما للتصور فمنه ما يفيد التمييز الذاتي؛ وهو إما الحد التام، أو الحد الناقص. ومنه ما يفيد التمييز العرضي وهو إما الرسم التام، أو الرسم الناقص. ثم الحــد إذا عرف بأنه قول وجـيـر مميز للمطلوب بالذات فذلك تعريف الحد مطلقا لا تعريف الحد التام والناقص داخل فيه، ولا تعريف الحد الناقص كذلك؛ والتام داخل فيه بل الحد يعرف كما عرفه المعلم الأول في كتاب الجدل: «إنه قول دال على الماهية؛ يعني بالماهية كمال حقيقة الشيء التي بها هو » (۱) وهذا هو الحد التام كما مر . فهذا الكتاب وهو كتاب البرهان يفيدنا المواد التي إذا جعلت حدود قياس كان القياس مفيدًا لليقين؛ وهو البرهاني كما مر ، ويفيدنا المواد التي إذا جعلت أجزاء الحد كان الحد مفيدًا للتصور التام. ويصلح أن يجعل التصور بنوع ما مبدأ للتصديق لأن كل مصدق به متصور، وليس كل متصور بمصدق به. فإن معاني الألفاظ المفردة والألفاظ المركبة التي ليس تركيبها تركيب قول جازم كلها متصور، وليست بمصدقة بل الأقوال الجازمة قد تكون متصورة ومصدقا بها كذلك. وذلك على وجهين: إما التصور فمن جهة أن معناها قائم في النفس كقولك الإنسان حيوان، وإما التصديق فلأن معناها مضاف إلى حال الشيء في نفسه بأنه كما تصور أي أنه كما حصلت منه صورة معقولة من نسبة أوقعت بين حديها مطابقة لحال حديها في الوجود وفي نفس الأمر . وإذا كان كذلك فيشبه أن يكون التصديق بوجه ما كالتمام للتصور. ويكون سائر أصناف التصورات التي لا تنفع في التصديق ومطرحة في العلوم. وإنما بطلت منها في العلوم ما يعين في التصديق؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون هذا الكتاب منسوبًا إلى القياس دون الحد؛ ولهذا سمي كتاب البرهان فأما في الحقيقة فهو كتاب البرهان والحد معا ولما عرفت فرض الكتاب فقد ظهرت منفعة الكتاب وهي التوصل إلى العلوم اليقينية والتصورات الحقيقية النافعة بتوسط استعمال هذه الآلة؛ وهي المنطق.
As for conception, among it is that which provides essential distinction; it is either perfect definition or imperfect definition. And among it is that which provides accidental distinction; it is either perfect description or imperfect description. Then definition, if defined as a brief statement distinguishing the sought by essence, then that is a definition of definition absolutely, not a definition of perfect definition and imperfect definition included in it, nor a definition of imperfect definition likewise; rather the perfect is included in it. Rather definition is defined as the First Teacher defined it in the Book of Dialectic: 'It is a statement indicating the essence; by essence he means the complete reality of the thing by which it is what it is.' And this is the perfect definition as previously mentioned. So this book, which is the Book of Demonstration, provides us with the materials which, if made into the terms/limits of a syllogism, the syllogism becomes productive of certainty; and that is the demonstrative syllogism as previously mentioned. And it provides us with the materials which, if made into parts of a definition, the definition becomes productive of complete conception. And it is valid to make conception, in a certain way, a principle for assent, because everything assented to is conceived, but not everything conceived is assented to. For the meanings of simple expressions and compound expressions whose composition is not a composition of a categorical statement are all conceived, and they are not assented to. Rather categorical statements may be both conceived and assented to. And that is in two ways: either conception, from the perspective that its meaning is present in the soul, like your saying 'Man is an animal'; or assent, because its meaning is added to the state of the thing in itself as it is conceived, i.e., that just as a conceptual form has resulted from it from a relation placed between its two terms, corresponding to the state of its two terms in existence and in reality. And if that is the case, then assent is, in a certain respect, like the completion of conception. And the rest of the kinds of conceptions that are not useful for assent are discarded in the sciences. Rather, only from among them what aids in assent is accepted in the sciences. Then it follows that this book is attributed to syllogism rather than definition; for this reason it is called the Book of Demonstration. But in reality, it is the book of demonstration and definition together. And when the purpose of the book is understood, the benefit of the book becomes apparent: it is the attainment of certain sciences and true beneficial conceptions through the use of this tool, which is logic.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the types of definitions and descriptions, establishing that the Book of Demonstration (Organon's Posterior Analytics) serves both for constructing demonstrative syllogisms that yield certainty and for providing complete definitions that yield perfect conception. The author begins by dividing what provides distinction in conception: essential distinction is achieved by definition (limit/hadd), either perfect (hadd tam) or imperfect (hadd naqis); accidental distinction is achieved by description (rasm), either perfect (rasm tam) or imperfect (rasm naqis). He then clarifies that the general definition of 'definition' as a brief statement distinguishing by essence covers both perfect and imperfect definitions, but the perfect definition is more specific. He cites Aristotle (the First Teacher) from his Topics, defining perfect definition as 'a statement indicating the essence', meaning the complete reality of the thing. Next, he explains that this book, the Book of Demonstration, provides the materials (premises) for demonstrative syllogisms and the components for definitions. He then discusses the relationship between conception and assent: all assent relies on prior conception, but not every conception leads to assent. Simple terms and non-assertoric compounds are only conceived, while categorical statements (jāzim) can be both conceived and assented to. Assent adds a relation of correspondence to reality, making it the completion of conception. Therefore, conceptions not useful for assent are disregarded in the sciences. Since the book's primary goal is assent through demonstration, it is named after demonstration, but it equally serves definition. In Islamic logic, this parallels how the sciences (e.g., fiqh) require both clear definitions of terms (e.g., what is 'prayer') and certain proofs (e.g., that prayer is obligatory).

Key Terms
الحد التام
الحد الناقص
الرسم التام
الرسم الناقص
تمييز ذاتي
تمييز عرضي

B) al-hadd al-naqis

Key Takeaway
Definitions are classified into essential vs. accidental and perfect vs. imperfect; this classification is essential for evaluating definitional precision in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conception and Assent: Dual Purpose of Demonstration
Author relates conception and assent and explains the book's dual role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conception (tasawwur) from assent (tasdiq) and explain why the Book of Demonstration serves both certain proof and complete definition.

Core Idea
Every assent requires prior conception; the Book of Demonstration provides the materials for both certain proof and complete definitions.

This passage highlights that all human knowledge begins with concepts, and without them, we cannot affirm truths. The Book of Demonstration therefore supplies both the premises for demonstrative syllogisms (to yield certainty) and the components for definitions (to yield perfect conception). Understanding this interdependence is crucial for any Islamic science that grounds its arguments in clear definitions and valid proofs.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish conception from assent
The passage defines tasawwur as simple apprehension (without judgment) and tasdiq as judgment that corresponds to reality. Understand that all assent involves conception of its terms.
Simple terms like 'Allah' are only conceived; statements like 'Allah is one' involve both.
2
Recognise the dependence
Note the key statement: 'everything assented to is conceived, but not everything conceived is assented to.' This hierarchy shows that conception is foundational for assent.
This is why clear definitions are necessary before any judgment.
3
Identify the two components the book provides
The Book of Demonstration supplies: (a) materials that become premises for syllogisms (to produce assent/certainty) and (b) materials that become parts of definitions (to produce complete conception).
These correspond to the two tasks of logic: concept formation and validation.
4
Explain the dual naming
The passage says the book is called 'Demonstration' because the ultimate goal is assent through certain syllogisms, but in reality it equally serves definition. Reflect on why both are necessary in Islamic sciences like kalam.
In proving God's existence, you need both definition of 'contingent' and a valid syllogism.
