Principles, Subjects, and Problems: The Anatomy of a Science
Author defines and distinguishes the three components of every science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principles, subjects, and problems of any given Islamic science text.

Core Idea
Every science is built on three pillars: principles (mabadi'), subject (mawdu'), and problems (masa'il).

The author now classifies the internal structure of every science, especially the theoretical ones. Understanding this tripartite division is essential for correctly reading any Islamic disciplinary work, whether in kalam, fiqh, or usul al-fiqh. The passage defines each component and explains their logical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Find the three Arabic terms (المبادئ, الموضوعات, المسائل) in the passage. Note their definitions: principles are the definitions and premises used for proof; subjects are the things whose essential accidents are investigated; problems are propositions whose predicates are essential accidents, doubted and sought to be proven.
2
Understand the reasons principles are not proven within the science
The author lists three distinct reasons: (1) self-evident clarity, (2) high status requiring a higher science, (3) low status proven in a lower science but without circularity. Distinguish these three.
Circularity (دور) is explicitly avoided by ensuring that the premises of the lower science do not depend on the principles of the higher science.
3
Grasp the logical relationship between the three
The author says: principles are that from which the demonstration is (منها البرهان), problems are that for which the demonstration is (لها البرهان), and subjects are that upon which the demonstration is (عليها البرهان). This triadic relation shows the function of each component in the process of proof.
4
Apply to a real Islamic science
Consider the science of kalam: its subject is the Essence of Allah and attributes; its principles include axioms like 'the world is originated' and 'everything originated has a cause'; its problems are conclusions like 'the Creator exists'. This structure is mirrored in works like al-Sanusiyya.
The same tripartite division applies to usul al-fiqh, nahw, and other Islamic disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢.٥. ] النوع الثاني : في موضوعات العلوم ومبادئها ومسائلها وافتراق مسائلها ومبادئها في حدودها المحمولة
ثم لكل واحد من الصناعات وخصوصا النظرية مبادئ وموضوعات ومسائل؛ أما المبادئ فهو الحدود والمقدمات التي تبرهن بها تلك الصناعة ولا تبرهن هي في تلك الصناعة: إما لوضوحها نحو البديهيات وأمثالها، وإما لعلو شأنها عن أن يبرهن فيها؛ وإنما يبرهن في علم فوقها، وإما لدنو شأنها عن أن يبرهن في تلك الصناعة بل إنما يبرهن فيما دونها لكن بمقدمات لا تتوقف صحتها عليها كيلا يلزم الدور؛ وهذا قليل
وأما الموضوعات فهي الأشياء التي إنما تبحث الصناعة عن الأحوال المنسوبة إليها والعوارض الذاتية لها.
وأما المسائل فهي القضايا التي محمولاتها عوارض ذاتية لموضوع هذه الصناعة أو لأنواعه أو لأعراضه؛ وهي مشكوك فيها ومبحوث عنها فيطلب برهانها في هذه الصناعة. فالمبادئ منها البرهان والمسائل لها البرهان والموضوعات عليها البرهان؛ والغرض فيما عليه البرهان الأعراض الذاتية، والذي لأجله ذلك هو الموضوع.
ثم لكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة أعني المبادئ والموضوعات والمسائل مباحث متعلقة به، فنقول: أما الحدود التي هي المبادئ فهي حدود موضوع
[2.5] The second type: On the subjects of the sciences, their principles, their problems, and the differentiation of their problems and principles in their predicative definitions.
Then each of the arts, especially the theoretical ones, has principles, subjects, and problems. As for the principles, they are the definitions and premises by which that art is demonstrated, and they themselves are not demonstrated within that art: either due to their clarity like the self-evidents and their likes, or due to their high status that they cannot be demonstrated therein; rather they are demonstrated in a science above it, or due to their low status that they cannot be demonstrated in that art, rather they are demonstrated in what is below it, but with premises whose correctness does not depend on them so that circularity does not occur; and this is rare.
As for the subjects, they are the things about which the art only investigates the states attributed to them and their essential accidents.
As for the problems, they are the propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of the subject of this art or of its species or of its accidents; they are doubted and investigated, so their demonstration is sought within this art. So, the principles are that from which the demonstration is, the problems are that for which the demonstration is, and the subjects are that upon which the demonstration is. The purpose of that upon which the demonstration is is the essential accidents, and that for the sake of which that exists is the subject.
Then each of these three, namely the principles, subjects, and problems, has investigations related to it. We say: As for the definitions which are the principles, they are the definitions of the subject...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the structural components of any science ('صناعة'), especially theoretical sciences. The author identifies three parts: principles (مبادئ), subjects (موضوعات), and problems (مسائل). Principles are the definitions and premises used to prove statements within the science; they themselves are not proven within that science for three reasons: they are self-evident (بديهيات), or they belong to a higher science and are proven there, or they belong to a lower science and are proven there without circularity (to avoid دور). Subjects are the things whose states and essential accidents the science investigates. Problems are propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of the subject, its species, or its accidents; these are doubted and require proof. The author then gives a tripartite relation: principles are that from which the proof comes (مِنها البرهان), problems are that for which the proof exists (لها البرهان), and subjects are that upon which the proof is built (عليها البرهان). The goal of the proof on the subject is to establish essential accidents, and the subject itself is the ultimate purpose. Finally, he notes that each of these three has its own investigations, and begins discussing definitions as principles. This passage provides a foundational taxonomy for understanding how Islamic sciences are structured logically.

الموضوعات
المسائل
العرض الذاتي

Key Takeaway
The logical structure of any demonstrative science is defined by its principles (mabadi'), subject (mawdu'), and problems (masa'il), each serving a distinct role in the process of proof.

Essential vs Accidental Predication
Author defines and distinguishes essential and accidental predication with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential predication (haml bi al-dhat) from accidental predication (haml bi al-'arad) in a given proposition and identify the true subject.

Core Idea
Essential predication ties the predicate to the substance; accidental predication ties it through an accident.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction: when we say 'the human is white', is 'white' truly predicated of 'human'? Or is it accidental? This distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions and arguments in Islamic logic, as it determines whether a predicate belongs to the subject by itself or through an intermediary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the example 'The human is white' – identify the subject (human) and predicate (white). Is the subject a substance (jawhar) that can stand by itself, or is it an accident that needs a substrate?
The passage states that 'human' is a substance not needing a substrate, unlike whiteness.
2
Check the subject's nature
If the subject is a substance, the predication is essential. If the subject is an accident (like 'white' in 'white is human'), the predication is accidental because the accident cannot be a true subject.
'White' in itself cannot be a subject; the real subject is the thing that has whiteness.
3
Apply the reversal test
Swap subject and predicate: 'White is human' vs. 'Human is white'. The reversed version is accidental because 'white' is not a substance. The passage uses this to illustrate accidental predication.
4
Distinguish essential by self vs. by nature
The passage gives two senses of essential predication: (a) when the description belongs to the thing by itself (e.g., 'the stone is moving' even if forced), and (b) when it is from its nature (e.g., 'the stone moves downward'). Both are essential because the stone itself is the subject.
Accidental predication in contrast: 'the passenger in the ship is moving' – motion belongs to the ship, not the passenger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال من وجه المحمول إنه محمول بالحقيقة لا بالعرض إذا كان الموضوع مستحقا لأن يوضع بذاته كما في قولنا الإنسان أبيض فإن الإنسان جوهر غير محتاج إلى حامل بخلاف البياض؛ وهذا هـو حمل حقيقي لا بالعرض. وبإزاء هذا حمل ما بالعرض كما في قولنا أبيض ما إنسان فيكون بالحقيقة الموضوع مأخوذا مرَّتين بالقوة. وذلك لأن الأبيض من حيث هو أبيض ما فقط لا يمكن أن يكون موضوعا في طباعه؛ إذ الموضوع هو الشيء الذي له البياض وهو الإنسان في هذه الصورة. وأما إذا كان عرضًا في واحد فيحمل أحدهما على الآخر فيقال إن الأبيض متحرك أي الذي له البياض له الحركة؛ ويقال للشيء إنه محمول بالذات والحقيقة إذا كان الوصف له بنفسه كما يقال إن الحجر متحرك وإن كان يتحرك بالقسر. وبإزاء هذا ما يحمل بالعرض كما يقال للساكن في السفينة إنه متحرك؛ ويقال للشيء إنه محمول بالذات والحقيقة أيضًا إذا كان شيء من طبعه كما يقال إن الحجر متحرك إلى أسفل بالذات. وبإزائه ما يحمل بالعرض كالمتحرك إلى فوق بالقسر.
It is said from the perspective of the predicate that it is predicated truly and not accidentally when the subject deserves to be posited by itself, as in our saying 'The human is white' – the human is a substance not needing a substrate, unlike whiteness; this is a true predication, not accidental. In contrast is predication by accident, as in our saying 'A white thing is a human' – then truly the subject is taken twice in potentiality. This is because 'white', insofar as it is merely white, cannot be a subject in its nature; for the subject is the thing that has whiteness, and it is the human in this example. And if an accident is in one thing, then one of them is predicated of the other, so it is said 'The white is moving,' meaning the thing that has whiteness has motion. And a thing is said to be predicated essentially and truly if the description belongs to it by itself, as it is said 'The stone is moving' even if it moves by force; in contrast is what is predicated accidentally, as it is said of the one sitting in a ship that he is moving. And a thing is also said to be predicated essentially and truly if it is something from its nature, as it is said 'The stone moves downward essentially'; in contrast is what is predicated accidentally, like moving upward by force.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between essential (bi al-dhat) and accidental (bi al-'arad) predication. The author explains that a predicate is truly (bi al-haqiqa) predicated, not accidentally, when the subject is a substance that can stand by itself (like 'human' in 'human is white') because the subject does not need a substrate, whereas whiteness is an accident that needs a subject. The reverse order ('white is human') is accidental predication because 'white' in itself cannot be a subject; the real subject is the substance that has whiteness (the human). Next, the author discusses cases where an accident is in one subject and another accident is predicated of it: e.g., 'the white is moving' means the thing that has whiteness has motion – here 'white' is used accidentally. Furthermore, a predicate is essential if the description belongs to the thing by itself, as when we say 'the stone is moving' even if moved by force (because motion is predicated of the stone itself, not accidentally). In contrast, saying the passenger in a ship 'is moving' is accidental because the motion belongs to the ship, not the person. Finally, essential predication also applies when the description is from the thing's nature, like 'the stone moves downward essentially' – downward motion is natural to stone; moving upward by force is accidental.

حمل بالعرض
موضوع
محمول

Key Takeaway
Essential predication occurs when the predicate applies to the subject as a substance or as a self-contained subject; accidental predication occurs when the predicate applies through an intermediary accident.

Finitude of Essential Predicates via Definitions
Author argues that essential predicates are finite because definitions are finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that essential predicates are finite because definitions are finite, and apply this reasoning to any definition in the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Definitions are finite, so the predicates they use must also be finite.

How do we know that the predicates which enter into the essence of a thing (genus, species, differentia, etc.) are not infinite? One powerful argument is based on the nature of definitions themselves. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why finitude is necessary for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that the predicates entering the essence (al-mahmulat al-dakhila fi al-mahiya) are finite (mutanahiya). Locate this claim in the Arabic.
2
Find the evidence for definitions
The author says these predicates are used in definitions (hudud), and definitions are completed by them. So if definitions exist, the predicates must exist and be finite.
3
Examine the conditional argument
The author states: if definitions were dependent on something infinite, then no definition could exist. But definitions do exist (we have knowledge of essences). Therefore, the principles (mabadi') of definitions must be finite.
This is a modus tollens argument: If infinite, then no definitions; definitions exist; so not infinite.
4
Connect to earlier conclusions
This argument reinforces the conclusion about the finitude of predication chains. It shows that the five universals, which are the building blocks of definitions, are limited in number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل أيضًا إن المحمولات الداخلة في ماهية الشيء متناهية لأن هذه داخلة في تحديد الأشياء؛ والحدود إنما تتم بها فلو كانت الحدود متوقعة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فلا وجود للحدود أصلا بل الحدود موجودة؛ إذ الأمور متصورة فمبادئها متناهية.
It has also been said that the predicates entering into the essence of a thing are finite because these enter into the definition of things; and definitions are only completed by them. So if definitions were dependent on something infinite, there would be no existence of definitions at all, but definitions are existent; for things are conceptualized, so their principles are finite.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents a separate argument for the finitude of essential predicates (i.e., the genus, species, differentia, etc.). The reasoning is that these predicates are part of definitions (hudud). Definitions are composed of finite components; otherwise, no definition could be completed, and we would not have knowledge of essences. Yet we do have definitions—since we conceptualize things—so the principles (mabadi') of definitions must be finite. This reinforces the earlier conclusion about the finitude of predication chains.

Key Terms
محمولات داخلة في الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must essential predicates be finite according to this argument?
A) Because they are all accidents.
B) Because they are used in definitions, and definitions are finite.
C) Because there are only five essential predicates.
D) Because they are all substances.

Key Takeaway
Essential predicates are finite because definitions, which rely on them, are actually attainable and finite.

Definitions of Industry, Wisdom, and Mental Faculties
Author defines industry, wisdom, and mental faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the definitions of industry, wisdom, and the five mental faculties as presented in the passage, and identify their genus and differentia where applicable.

Core Idea
The logical activity of the mind is defined through six precise faculties: mind, understanding, intuition, intelligence, and thought.

This passage presents foundational definitions for understanding the psychology of knowledge acquisition. It begins with industry (sina'a) and wisdom (hikma), then details the mental faculties that facilitate logical reasoning. These definitions are crucial for later discussions on how definitions and syllogisms are formed in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'industry' (sina'a)
Find the sentence starting with 'Ammā al-sinā‘atu...'. Note the genus: 'psychical faculty' (malaka nafsāniyya) and the differentia: 'from which voluntary actions issue without deliberation'.
This is a complete definition (hadd) with a clear genus and differentia.
2
Analyze the two aspects of wisdom (hikma)
Wisdom is defined as 'the soul's exit to its possible perfection in the two limits of knowledge and action'. The theoretical side is conceiving existence and affirming propositions as they are; the practical side is acquiring the character of justice. Note that this is called 'human wisdom'.
This is a descriptive definition (rasm) rather than a strict hadd.
3
List the five mental faculties
The first is mind (dhihn) as a receptive power for definitions and opinions. Then understanding (fahm) as readiness to conceive what comes from others. Intuition (hads) as active movement to seize the middle term independently. Intelligence (dhakā') as rapid intuition. Thought (fikr) as movement from principles to demands and back.
Each faculty builds upon the previous: mind is the base, understanding is readiness, intuition is active, intelligence is speed, and thought is directed movement.
4
Compare intuition (hads) and thought (fikr)
Intuition directly seizes the middle term without a lengthy process, while thought is a deliberate movement seeking that middle term. Both are movements of the mind, but intuition is quicker and more direct. The example of observing the moon's illumination illustrates intuition.
This distinction is important for understanding different modes of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في الصناعة وغيرها فذلك لا يكون من هذا العلم بل من الطبيعيات الخلقية غير أن التعرُّض لتعريفها في هذا الموضع على وفق الحكمة فنقول أما الصناعة فإنها ملكة نفسانية تصدر عنها أفعال اختيارية من غير روية، والحكمة خروج نفس الإنسان إلى كماله الممكن له في حدي العلم والعمل؛ أما من جانب العلم فإن يكون متصورًا للموجود كما هي ومصدقا بالقضايا كما هي، وأما من جانب العمل فإن يكون قد حصل عنده الخلق الذي يسمى العدالة: وهذه هي الحكمة الإنسانية لا نفس الحكمة. والذهن قوة للنفس متهيئة ومستعدة لاكتساب الحدود والآراء، والفهم جودة تهيؤ لهذه القوة نحو تصور ما يرد عليها من غيرها، والحدس جودة حركة لهذه القوة إلى اقتناص الحدّ الأوسط من تلقاء نفسها مثل أن يرى الإنسان القمر إنما يضيء دائما من جانبه الذي يلي الشمس على أشكاله فيقتنض ذهنه بحدسه حدا أوسط وهو أن سببه من الشمس والذكاء جودة حدس من هذه القوة يقع في زمان قصير غير مهمل، والفكر حركة ذهن الإنسان نحو المبادئ للمطالب ليرجع إلى المطالب.
As for speech about industry and other things, that is not part of this science but rather from natural created things, except that presenting their definitions in this place is in accordance with wisdom. So we say: Industry is a psychical faculty from which voluntary actions issue without deliberation. Wisdom is the soul's exit to its possible perfection in the two limits of knowledge and action. As for the side of knowledge, it is that one conceives existence as it is and affirms propositions as they are. As for the side of action, it is that one has acquired the character which is called justice. This is human wisdom, not wisdom itself. The mind is a power of the soul prepared and ready for acquiring definitions and opinions. Understanding is the quality of readiness of this power towards conceiving what comes to it from others. Intuition is the quality of movement of this power to seize the middle term from itself, such as when a person sees that the moon only illuminates always from its side facing the sun according to its shapes, so his mind by his intuition seizes a middle term which is that its cause is from the sun. Intelligence is the quality of intuition of this power occurring in a short time without neglect. Thought is the movement of the human mind towards the principles for the demands in order to return to the demands.

Commentary
This passage provides definitions of key terms in the theory of knowledge acquisition. The author first notes that the discussion of industry (sina'a) belongs to natural sciences but defines it here as a psychical faculty from which voluntary actions issue without deliberation. Wisdom (hikma) is the soul's reaching its possible perfection in both theoretical and practical realms: theoretically by conceiving beings as they are and affirming propositions as they are, practically by acquiring the virtue of justice. Then he defines mental faculties: mind (dhihn) is a receptive power for acquiring definitions and opinions; understanding (fahm) is the readiness to conceive incoming ideas; intuition (hads) is the movement of this power to seize the middle term independently, as when observing the moon's illumination; intelligence (dhaka') is quick intuition; thought (fikr) is the movement of the mind from principles to sought conclusions. These definitions set the psychological stage for logical inquiry.

Distinction Between Demonstration and Definition
Author argues demonstration and definition are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differences between demonstration (burhan) and definition (hadd) and explain why not every middle term in a demonstration is a definition.

Core Idea
Demonstration gives the 'why' via efficient causes; definition gives the 'what' via essential causes.

In this passage, the author argues that demonstration and definition are fundamentally different, despite some overlap. You will learn to distinguish between the causes that belong to definitions (essential causes) and those that belong to demonstrations (efficient causes), and you will see three clear differences between the two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that not all middle terms in demonstrations are definitions. Locate where he says this and note the examples he gives (e.g., fire touching wood).
The claim is crucial to avoid conflating logical tools.
2
Examine the example of fire touching wood
Why is 'touching fire' not a definition of burning? Because it is an efficient cause (cause of existence), not an essential cause (form or matter).
3
Understand the distinction between 'illa fa'iliyya and 'illa dhatiyya
Efficient causes bring about existence; essential causes constitute quiddity. The author says efficient causes belong to descriptions (rusum), not definitions.
This distinction is key to the argument.
4
List the three differences between demonstration and definition
The passage enumerates: (1) Every definition is universal, not every demonstration; (2) Demonstration gives essential accidents ('arad dhati), definition gives constitutive essentials; (3) Fundamental premises are not demonstrated but defined.
These differences show they are separate methods.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a middle term from an Islamic science example, decide if it is part of a definition or an efficient cause. For instance, is 'intoxication' in the proof for wine's prohibition a definition or an efficient cause?
Practice on real examples solidifies understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن العلل الموجبة لأمور لا في أنفسها ولا بوجه ما نفس الموجب لا صورة ولا مادة وكثيرًا ما نجد من الأوساط في البراهين ما ليس مادة ولا صورة حدا بل نجد شيئًا موجباً لشيء في شيء فالجنس المتوسط يوجب وجود الجنس الأعلى في النوع الآخر بل وفي كل ما يحمل عليه الجنس المتوسط؛ وليس هو حدا للأكبر ولا صورة ولا مادة. وكثيرًا من الخواص هي علة لكثير من الخواص وهي خارجة عنها ليست بجنس لها ولا فصل ولا حد. وكثيرًا من الأوساط البرهانية ليست حدودًا ولا عللا داخلة في جوهر الشيء بل عللا فاعلية وموجبة وحدا حكم مماسة النار فإنها قد يجعل حدا أوسط في إثبات إحراق الخشبة وإن كان قد يمكن أن تجعل هذه العلة الموجبة فصولا من جهة على أنها أجزاء الفصول لا تحمل الفصول المعمولة منها كما أن القدوم لا يقال إنه حديد بل من حديد، ولا يقال إن الحمى عفونة بل من عفونة، وليست أجزاء فصول الذات هي أخص الفصول بل أجزاء فصول خاصة فقط. والعلل الفاعلية علل للوجود، وأجزاء الحد علل للماهية على ما عرف. ولهذا لا تدخل علل الوجود في الحدود بل في الرسوم القائمة مقام الحدود، فإذن ليس كل حد أوسط حدا أو جزء حد. ولو كان البرهان هو الذي أوسطه للحد فلا يوجد برهان على شيء إلا على ما وجود حد الأكبر للأصغر فيه ظاهر، ووجود نفس الحد الأكبر خفي. ثم البرهان بمعزل عن الحد وإن كان قد يتوهم فيما سلف من مشاركة طلب اللم وهو طلب البرهان، وطلب ما وهو طلب الحد أن البرهان والحدّ قد يقومان على شيء واحد من جهة واحدة؛ وليس كذلك [1] منها أن كل حد؛ وليس كل برهان كذلك، [ب] ومنها أن كل حد فمحدوده كلي؛ وليس كل برهان كذلك، [ت] ومنها أن البرهان يعطي للشيء عرضًا ذاتيا كما مر؛ والحد يعطي من الذاتيات ما يكون مقوما؛ فنفس إعطاء الحــد إذن ليس هو نفس إعطاء برهان وإن كان قد يتفق في كثير من الصور أن يشارك الحد البرهان في المادة لكن ليس كذلك دائما. فإن المقدمات الواجب قبولها لا برهان عليها؛ وأجزاؤها تعطي الحدود دون البرهان عليها لما أنها بسائط. ولو كان على كل شيء برهان لما كان على كل شيء برهان؛ وأنت تعلم أن الحد شيء غير البرهان، وأنه ليس كل محدود مبرهنـا بحده، ولا كل مبرهن محدودًا ببرهانه، وما يدل على أن هذا غير ذلك فذلك متعدد يعرف بالتأمل بعد الاطلاع عليهما لا محالة.
For the causes that necessitate things are not in themselves nor in any way the very necessitator is not form nor matter. And often we find among the middle terms in demonstrations what is neither matter nor form nor a definition. Rather we find something necessitating something in something: the intermediate genus necessitates the existence of the higher genus in the other species, and indeed in everything upon which the intermediate genus is predicated; and it is not a definition of the major term nor a form nor a matter. And many properties are causes of many properties, and they are external to them, not a genus for them, nor a differentia, nor a definition. And many demonstrative middle terms are not definitions nor causes internal to the essence of the thing, but efficient causes and necessitating and a definition? The ruling of the touching of fire, for it may be made a middle term in proving the burning of wood, although it may be possible to make these necessitating causes differentiae in some respect, on the basis that they are parts of the differentiae, not that the composite differentiae are predicated, just as the axe is not said to be iron but from iron, and fever is not said to be putrefaction but from putrefaction. And the parts of the differentiae of the essence are not the most specific differentiae but only parts of specific differentiae. And efficient causes are causes of existence, and parts of the definition are causes of quiddity as known. For this reason, causes of existence do not enter into definitions but into descriptions that stand in place of definitions. Therefore, not every middle term is a definition or part of a definition. And if demonstration were that whose middle term is the definition, then there would be no demonstration of anything except what has the existence of the definition of the major for the minor apparent, and the existence of the definition itself hidden. Then demonstration is separate from definition, although it may have been imagined earlier that the request for 'why' which is the request for demonstration, and the request for 'what' which is the request for definition, that demonstration and definition may be based on one thing from one aspect; but it is not so. [1] One [difference] is that every definition... and not every demonstration is so. [B] Another is that every definition has its definiendum universal; and not every demonstration is so. [C] Another is that demonstration gives the thing an accidental essential as passed; and definition gives from the essential what is constitutive. So the giving of definition itself is not the same as the giving of demonstration, although it may happen in many forms that definition shares with demonstration in matter, but not always. For the premises that must be accepted have no demonstration; and their parts give definitions without demonstration because they are simples. And if everything had a demonstration, then not everything would have a demonstration. And you know that definition is something other than demonstration, and that not every definiendum is demonstrated by its definition, nor every demonstrated thing is defined by its demonstration. And what indicates that this is other than that are multiple things known by reflection after examining them inevitably.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument distinguishing demonstration (burhan) from definition (hadd). The author begins by explaining that not every middle term in a demonstration is a definition or part of a definition. Some middle terms are causes of existence (efficient causes) rather than causes of quiddity (formal/essential causes). For example, the touch of fire is a middle term proving burning, but it is not an essential part of the definition of burning; it is an efficient cause. The author distinguishes between causes of existence (which go into descriptive definitions, rusum) and causes of quiddity (which go into essential definitions, hudud). Therefore, the claim that demonstration's middle term is always the definition is false. Then the author explicitly states that demonstration is separate from definition, contrary to a possible misunderstanding. He enumerates three differences: (1) Every definition is universal (since it gives the essence of a universal), but not every demonstration is universal (some demonstrations are of particulars). (2) Every definition has a universal definiendum, but not every demonstration is of a universal. (3) Demonstration gives essential accidents (accidents that are necessary concomitants), while definition gives constitutive essentials. Thus, the act of definition is not the same as demonstration, even if they sometimes share the same matter. He concludes by noting that fundamental premises cannot be demonstrated, and their parts (simple terms) are defined without demonstration. The passage ends by affirming that definition and demonstration are distinct, and reflection reveals many indicators of this difference.

Why Definition Cannot Be Acquired by Demonstration
Author proves definition not by demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument that definition cannot be proved by demonstration and identify the key condition of convertibility.

Core Idea
Definition and definiendum must be convertible; no demonstration can guarantee this.

In this passage, the author begins a new section on definition (hadd). He asserts that definition cannot be acquired by demonstration (burhan), division (qisma), or induction (istiqra'), but only by composition (tarkib). He then proves why demonstration fails: it would require the middle term to be coextensive with both the definition and the definiendum, which leads to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author claims that definition is not acquired by demonstration. Focus on the first sentence: 'ثم إن الحد لا يكتسب بالبرهان'.
2
Extract the condition for demonstration
If definition were by demonstration, the middle term (al-awsat) would have to be coextensive (musawi) with both extremes: the definition (major) and the definiendum (minor). This is necessary for a valid demonstration of a definition.
Coextensivity means they have the same extension.
3
Analyze the alternative cases
The author shows what happens if the middle term is not coextensive. He considers two possibilities: more general than the minor or more specific than the major. He argues that both lead to the definition being more general than the definiendum, which is impossible because definition and definiendum must be convertible (mun'akis).
4
Reconstruct the reductio
Assume definition is by demonstration. Then the middle term must be coextensive. But if it is not, we get a contradiction. Even if it were coextensive, the structure of demonstration would force the definition to be more general. Hence, the assumption leads to impossibility, so definition cannot be by demonstration.
The text cuts off at the second case, but the overall proof is clear.
5
Identify the logical form
The argument is a modus tollens: If definition were by demonstration (P), then the middle term would be coextensive (Q). But coextensivity leads to impossibility (not Q). Therefore, not P.
This is a conditional syllogism of the istithna'i type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الحد لا يكتسب بالبرهان ولا بالقسمة والاستقراء بل بطريق التركيب، أما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون اكتسابه بالبرهان فذلك أنه إذا كان بالبرهان كان الأوسط مساويًا للطرفين أعني الحد الذي هو الأكبر والمحدود الذي هو الأصغر وإلا لكان إما أعم من الأصغر أو أخص من الأكبر لاستحالة أن يكون على العكس. وقد كان لا يمكن أن يكون المحمول في القضية الكلية أخص من موضوعها؛ وإنما كان يلزم أن يكون الحد أعم من المحدود، وذلك محال فإن كل واحد من الحد والمحدود يجب أن يكون منعكسا إلى الآخر. أما إذا كان أعم من الأصغر فلأن الأكبر حينئذ إما أن يكون أعم من الأوسط أو مساويًا له؛ ويلزم من لزوم أيهما كان أن يكون أعم من الأصغر فإن الأعم من الأعم أعم. وكذلك المساوي للأعم. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللزوم أن يكون الحد أعم من المحدود. وأما إذا كان أخص من الأكبر فلأن الأوسط
Then definition is not acquired by demonstration nor by division and induction, but by the method of composition. As for its not being possible to acquire it by demonstration, that is because if it were by demonstration, the middle term would be coextensive with the two extremes, namely the definition which is the major and the definiendum which is the minor; otherwise it would be either more general than the minor or more specific than the major, because it is impossible for it to be the opposite. And it would not be possible for the predicate in a universal proposition to be more specific than its subject; rather it would necessarily follow that the definition is more general than the definiendum, and that is impossible, for each of the definition and the definiendum must be convertible with the other. As for if it is more general than the minor, then the major in that case is either more general than the middle or equal to it; and from either case it follows that it is more general than the minor, for the more general than the general is more general. And likewise the equal to the general. And if it were so, then it would necessarily follow that the definition is more general than the definiendum. As for if it is more specific than the major, because the middle...
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage begins a new section: 'The third type: Inquiries related to definition.' The author states that definition is not acquired by demonstration, division, or induction, but by composition (tarkib). He then provides a proof by impossibility: if definition were acquired by demonstration, the middle term in that demonstration would have to be coextensive (musawi) with both extremes — the definition (major term) and the definiendum (minor term). If it were not coextensive, it would be either more general than the minor (definiendum) or more specific than the major (definition). But it cannot be the opposite (i.e., more specific than the minor or more general than the major) because in a universal proposition, the predicate cannot be more specific than the subject. So if the middle is more general than the minor, then the major (definition) would be either more general than the middle or equal to it. In either case, the major would be more general than the minor (since what is more general than the more general is itself more general, and what is equal to the more general is also more general than the minor). That would make the definition more general than the definiendum, which is impossible because definition and definiendum must be convertible. Similarly, if the middle is more specific than the major, then... (the text cuts off). The argument shows that definition cannot be acquired through demonstration because the required relation of coextension cannot be maintained.

Distinguishing Definition from Demonstration
Author enumerates multiple differences between definition and demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition (hadd) and demonstration (burhan) by identifying what each gives and their scope.

Core Idea
Definition gives the constitutive essence; demonstration gives an essential accident.

In this passage, the author enumerates several differences between defining a thing and demonstrating something about it. While definition and demonstration sometimes share subject matter, they are fundamentally different logical operations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding confusion when analyzing propositions in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
The passage contrasts hadd (definition) and burhan (demonstration). Locate where each is mentioned and what the author says about each.
2
List the differences stated
The author gives at least three differences: (1) every definition has a universal definiendum, not every demonstration; (2) demonstration gives an essential accident, definition gives constitutive essentials; (3) first principles are not demonstrated but defined.
Look for the enumeration markers [ب] and [ت] in the Arabic.
3
Understand constitutive vs. accidental
Constitutive essentials (muqawwimat) are part of the essence (e.g., genus and differentia). Essential accidents (arad dhati) are necessary attributes that follow from the essence but are not part of it (e.g., the ability to laugh for humans).
4
Reflect on epistemological implications
The author argues that not everything can be demonstrated; foundational premises must be accepted without proof. This shows the role of definition in providing simple terms from which demonstrations are built.
Consider how this relates to axiomatic knowledge in Islamic sciences.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد؛ وليس كل برهان كذلك، [ب] ومنها أن كل حد فمحدوده كلي؛ وليس كل برهان كذلك، [ت] ومنها أن البرهان يعطي للشيء عرضًا ذاتيا كما مر؛ والحد يعطي من الذاتيات ما يكون مقوما؛ فنفس إعطاء الحــد إذن ليس هو نفس إعطاء برهان وإن كان قد يتفق في كثير من الصور أن يشارك الحد البرهان في المادة لكن ليس كذلك دائما. فإن المقدمات الواجب قبولها لا برهان عليها؛ وأجزاؤها تعطي الحدود دون البرهان عليها لما أنها بسائط. ولو كان على كل شيء برهان لما كان على كل شيء برهان؛ وأنت تعلم أن الحد شيء غير البرهان، وأنه ليس كل محدود مبرهنـا بحده، ولا كل مبرهن محدودًا ببرهانه، وما يدل على أن هذا غير ذلك فذلك متعدد يعرف بالتأمل بعد الاطلاع عليهما لا محالة.
Definition; and not every demonstration is like that. [B] And among them is that every definition has its definiendum as a universal; and not every demonstration is like that. [T] And among them is that demonstration gives an essential accident to the thing as previously mentioned; and definition gives from the essentials what is constitutive. So the act of giving a definition is not the same as the act of giving a demonstration, even if it may agree in many forms that definition shares with demonstration in matter, but not always. For the premises that must be accepted have no demonstration; and their parts give definitions without demonstration upon them because they are simple. And if everything had a demonstration, then not everything would have a demonstration. And you know that definition is something other than demonstration, and that not every defined thing is demonstrated by its definition, nor every demonstrated thing defined by its demonstration. And what indicates that this is other than that is manifold, known by reflection after examining them without doubt.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage lists several differences between definition (hadd) and demonstration (burhan). The author begins by stating that definition is not always commensurate with demonstration. The first difference: every definition has a universal definiendum, while demonstration may have a particular conclusion. Second: demonstration gives an essential accident (عرض ذاتي), whereas definition gives the constitutive essentials (ذاتيات مقومة). The author then clarifies that despite occasional overlap in subject matter, the acts themselves are distinct. He notes that premises which must be accepted (i.e., first principles) are not demonstrated, and their simple parts are defined without proof. The argument that 'if everything had a demonstration, then not everything would have a demonstration' is a rhetorical proof by contradiction. The passage concludes by affirming that definition and demonstration are different, and that reflection confirms this.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The definition of a thing, composed of its constitutive essentials (muqawwimat). [Note: No significant shift — hadd is stable across the tradition.]
برهان (burhan): A demonstrative syllogism that gives an essential accident (arad dhati) of the thing. [Note: In earlier texts, burhan sometimes refers to any valid syllogism; here it specifically means demonstration yielding certain knowledge.]
عرض ذاتي (arad dhati): An attribute that is necessary but not constitutive of the essence, e.g., the ability to laugh for humans. [Note: Stable term, though some logicians distinguish between essential accidents and propria.]
مقوم (muqawwim): A constitutive essential (dhati) that forms part of the definition, such as genus and differentia. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is a difference between definition (hadd) and demonstration (burhan)?
A) Definition gives essential accidents, demonstration gives constitutive essentials.
B) Every definition has a universal definiendum, but not every demonstration is universal.
C) Demonstration always requires definition as a premise.
D) Definition and demonstration are the same in all respects.

Key Takeaway
Definition and demonstration are distinct: definition reveals the constitutive essence, while demonstration reveals a necessary accident, and not everything can be demonstrated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the definition of 'man' as 'rational animal' (hadd). Using the same concept, a demonstration might prove that man is capable of laughter (an essential accident). Identify which is definition and which is demonstration, and explain how they differ.

In a fiqh argument: 'Khamr is forbidden because it intoxicates.' Is the statement 'khamr is an intoxicant' part of its definition or a demonstration? Justify your answer using the criteria from this passage.

Why Definition Cannot Be Acquired by Demonstration
Author proves that definition is acquired by composition, not by demonstration, division, or induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that definition cannot be obtained by demonstration and identify the four cases that lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
Definition requires composition of essentials, not syllogistic proof.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational principle in Islamic logic: definition (hadd) is not learned through demonstrative syllogisms (burhan). He systematically eliminates demonstration by showing that any attempt to prove a definition leads to contradictions, circularity, or infinite regress. Instead, definition is built through composition (tarkib) of genus and differentia. Follow the steps below to trace this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis being proven
The passage begins by stating that definition is not acquired by demonstration, division, or induction, but only by composition. This is the claim the author will prove.
Note the contrast: burhan vs. tarkib.
2
Understand the role of the middle term
If definition were proven by demonstration, the middle term would have to be equal to both extremes (definition and definiendum). Otherwise, the definition would be broader or narrower, which is impossible because definition and definiendum must be convertible.
Convertibility means each implies the other (mutually inclusive).
3
Examine the four ways the middle could be predicated as a definition
The author lists four cases: (1) as a complete definition, (2) as an incomplete definition, (3) as a definition in whatever way, (4) as obtaining for everything the minor obtains for. Each case is refuted.
These are exhaustive possibilities for how the middle could serve as definition.
4
Trace the refutation of the first case as a model
For case 1: If the middle were a complete definition, one thing would have two complete definitions, impossible. Moreover, its being a definition would either depend on demonstration (leading to circularity or infinite regress) or not (leading to unequal evidentness among essentials).
This shows the impossibility at the core.
5
Conclude why definition cannot be from demonstration
Since all four cases fail, the only remaining method is composition (tarkib). The argument proves that definition is not acquired by demonstrative syllogism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : في المباحث المتعلقة بالحدّ
ثم إن الحد لا يكتسب بالبرهان ولا بالقسمة والاستقراء بــل بطريــق التركيب، أما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون اكتسابه بالبرهان فذلك أنه إذا كان بالبرهان كان الأوسط مساويًا للطرفين أعني الحد الذي هو الأكبر والمحدود الذي هو الأصغر وإلا لكان إما أعم من الأصغر أو أخص من الأكبر لاستحالة أن يكون على العكس. وقد كان لا يمكن أن يكون المحمول في القضية الكلية أخص من موضوعها؛ وإنما كان يلزم أن يكون الحد أعم من المحدود، وذلك محال فإن كل واحد من الحد والمحدود يجب أن يكون منعكسا إلى الآخر. أما إذا كان أعم من الأصغر فلأن الأكبر حينئذ إما أن يكون أعم من الأوسط أو مساويًا له؛ ويلزم من لزوم أيهما كان أن يكون أعم من الأصغر فإن الأعم من الأعم أعم. وكذلك المساوي للأعم. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللزوم أن يكون الحد أعم من المحدود. وأما إذا كان أخص من الأكبر فلأن الأوسط
حينئذ يكون مساويا للأصغر أو أعم منه دائما / كان يلزم أن يكون الأكبر أعم من الأصغر لما مرَّ فلا يلزم أن يكون الحد أعم من المحدود أيضًا.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن الأوسط المساوي للأصغر إذا حمل عليه على أنه حد له فإما أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد كامل، أو ناقص، أو لا على أنه هذا أو ذلك بل على أنه حد له كيف كان أو على أنه حاصل لكل حاصل للأصغر. [1] فإن كان الأول كان الحد الكامل للشيء الواحد اثنين؛ أحدهما هو الأوسط في إثبات الآخر له، وذلك محال فإن الحد الكامل مركب من جميع مقومات الشيء؛ والجميع لا يمكن أن يكون متعددا. وقد مر من قبل ما يدل على استحالة كونه وهو الحد الكامل متعددا، ولأن كونه حدا للأصغر إما أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى البرهان أو لا يكون فإن كان مفتقرًا إليه يلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل . وإن لم يكن يلزم أن يكون البعض من الحدود أجلى من البعض؛ وذلك محال؛ إذ الحدود كلها من الذاتيات فلا يمكن أن يكون بعضها أظهر بالنسبة إلى البعض. [٢] وإن كان الثاني وهو أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد ناقص فالكلام فيه نحو الكلام في الأول لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مفتقرا إلى البرهان أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يلزم المحال كما مر . [٣] وإن كان الثالث وهو أن يحمل عليه على ما به حد كيف كان فكذلك؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون كاملا على نفس الأمر أو ناقصا لاستحالة وجود ما يعمها بدون أحدهما؛ والكلام فيهما قد مر . [٤] وإن كان الرابع فالأكبر المساوي للأوسط إما أن يحمل عليه على أنه حد لنفسه، أو لكل ما حصل له الأول، أو حاصل لكل ما حصل له الأوسط، والكل باطل . [1] أما الأول فلأن الكلام فيه نحو الكلام في الأول. [ب] وأما الثاني فلأنه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول لأن ما حصل له الأوسط يلزم أن يكون هو المحدود فقط لامتناع أن يكون القول الواحد حدا للمختلفين
The third type: on investigations related to definition. Then indeed definition is not acquired by demonstration, nor by division and induction, but by the method of composition. As for its not being possible to acquire it by demonstration, that is because if it were by demonstration, the middle term would be equal to the two extremes, namely the definition which is the major term and the definiendum which is the minor term; otherwise it would be either broader than the minor or narrower than the major, since the reverse is impossible. And the predicate in a universal proposition cannot be narrower than its subject; rather it would necessitate that the definition be broader than the definiendum, and that is impossible, for each of the definition and the definiendum must be convertible with the other. As for if it is broader than the minor, then the major would be either broader than the middle or equal to it; and whichever it is, it would necessitate that it is broader than the minor, for what is broader than the broad is broad. And likewise what is equal to the broad. And if that were so, then it would be necessary that the definition is broader than the definiendum. And as for if it is narrower than the major, then the middle would be equal to the minor or always broader than it; it would necessitate that the major is broader than the minor as was mentioned, so it would not necessitate that the definition is also broader than the definiendum. If you understand this, we say: The middle term equal to the minor, if it is predicated of it as its definition, then either it is predicated as a complete definition, or an incomplete definition, or not as this or that but as a definition in whatever way, or as obtaining for everything that obtains for the minor. [1] If the first, then the complete definition of one thing would be two; one of them is the middle term in proving the other for it, and that is impossible, for the complete definition is composed of all the constitutives of the thing; and the total cannot be multiple. And what indicates the impossibility of its being multiple (the complete definition) has passed earlier, and because its being a definition of the minor either depends on demonstration or not; if it depends, then either circularity or infinite regress follows. And if not, then some definitions would be more evident than others; and that is impossible, for definitions are all from the essentials, so it is impossible that some are more manifest than others. [2] If the second, that it is predicated as an incomplete definition, then the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the first, because it must either depend on demonstration or not; and impossibility follows as before. [3] If the third, that it is predicated as a definition in whatever way, the same applies; because it must be either complete or incomplete in reality, since it is impossible for something to encompass both without one of them; and the discussion of these two has passed. [4] If the fourth, then the major equal to the middle is either predicated as a definition of itself, or of everything that the first obtains for, or as obtaining for everything that the middle obtains for, and all are false. [1] As for the first, the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the first. [B] As for the second, it is a petitio principii (begging the question) for the first claim, because what the middle obtains for must be only the definiendum, since it is impossible for one statement to be a definition for two different things.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a proof that definition (hadd) is not acquired by demonstration (burhan), division (qisma), or induction (istiqra'), but rather by composition (tarkib). The author begins with a logical analysis: if definition were obtained by demonstration, the middle term (al-awsat) in that syllogism would have to be equal to both extremes—the definition (major term) and the definiendum (minor term). If not, the middle would be broader than the minor or narrower than the major. The author examines these possibilities. If the middle is broader than the minor, then the major (definition) would be broader than the minor (definiendum), contradicting the requirement that definition and definiendum are convertible. If the middle is narrower than the major, then the major would be broader than the minor, but then the definition would not be broader than the definiendum—still problematic. Having established that the middle must equal the minor, the author considers how the middle (as a definition) is predicated of the minor. There are four cases: (1) as a complete definition, (2) as an incomplete definition, (3) as a definition in whatever way, (4) as obtaining for everything the minor obtains for. The author refutes each. For case (1), if the middle is a complete definition, then there would be two complete definitions for one thing (the middle and the definition itself), which is impossible. Moreover, the middle's being a definition would require either dependence on demonstration (leading to circularity or infinite regress) or not, which would imply some definitions are more evident than others, but all definitions are equally evident as essentials. Cases (2) and (3) reduce to similar arguments. Case (4) is examined with subcases: the major equal to the middle could be a definition of itself (redundant), of everything the minor obtains for (begging the question), or of what the middle obtains for (impossible). Thus, all possibilities fail, proving that definition cannot be acquired by demonstration.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A complete definition composed of all constitutive essentials of a thing, convertible with the definiendum. [Note: No significant shift.]
البرهان (al-burhan): A demonstrative syllogism that aims to prove an attribute of a thing, with a middle term that may not be equal to the extremes. [Note: In this text, burhan is contrasted with hadd specifically regarding the relationship of middle to extremes.]
الأوسط (al-awsat): The middle term in a syllogism; in this context, it is the term that would hypothetically serve as the definition in a demonstration. [Note: Standard syllogistic term; no shift.]
الأكبر (al-akbar): The major term (predicate of the conclusion); here it is the definition itself. [Note: Standard; no shift.]
الأصغر (al-asghar): The minor term (subject of the conclusion); here it is the definiendum. [Note: Standard; no shift.]
القسمة (al-qisma): Division, a method of classification used to find definitions, but rejected here as a source of definition. [Note: In other contexts, qisma is accepted as a tool for definition; here it is explicitly denied.]
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Induction, here rejected as a source of definition because it does not yield the essential constitutive framework. [Note: Standard; no shift.]
التركيب (al-tarkib): Composition, the method of combining genus and differentia to form a definition. [Note: Standard in definition theory.]
دور (dawr): Circular reasoning (vicious circle), one of the consequences if definition depended on demonstration. [Note: Standard logical fallacy; no shift.]
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress, another consequence of dependence on demonstration. [Note: Standard; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the middle term in a supposed demonstration of a definition is broader than the minor term (the definiendum), what follows according to the text?
A) The definition becomes narrower than the definiendum.
B) The definition becomes broader than the definiendum, which is impossible.
C) The syllogism becomes valid.
D) The definition and definiendum become equal.

Key Takeaway
Definition is not acquired through demonstration but through composition of essentials; any attempt to prove a definition syllogistically leads to absurdity.

Refutation of Acquiring Definition by Division
Author argues that definition cannot be obtained through logical division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why logical division fails to produce definitions and articulate the author's critique of the method.

Core Idea
Division presupposes the definition it seeks — it cannot produce new knowledge.

The author argues that the method of division (qisma) — splitting a genus by differentiate to isolate the definiens — is inherently circular. This lesson unpacks the critique: division yields indeterminate parts, relies on unproven premises, and ultimately begs the question by requiring prior knowledge of the definition. Understanding this critique sharpens our grasp of how definitions are actually known in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division method as described
The author outlines the process: dividing a genus into species (e.g., 'Man is either rational animal or irrational animal'), then eliminating the incorrect part. Note that this requires a determinate set of parts and a true conditional premise.
The example uses man: either rational animal or irrational animal; the latter is not the definition, so the former is taken as the definition.
2
Identify the first flaw: indeterminacy
Division yields indeterminate parts. To pick one part as the definition is to impose a prior determination, not derive it from division. The division itself does not specify which part is the definiens.
The division itself does not specify which part is the definiens.
3
Recognize the need for proof of exhaustiveness and truth
The exclusive disjunction used in division must be exhaustive (no missing parts) and the conditional premise must be true. The author asserts both need proof, which is not provided by division alone.
Example: 'Man is either animal or non-animal; but he is not non-animal, so he is animal' — the division 'animal or non-animal' must be exhaustive and the negation correct.
4
Expose the circularity involving the 'detailed statement'
The detailed statement (al-qawl al-mufassal) is claimed to be a definition of definition. Using it in a syllogism to prove a definition is begging the question, because it assumes what a definition is. One who denies that definitions are known only through demonstration will not accept that statement as a definition.
This is the core epistemological critique: the method relies on a meta-definition that itself requires justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون اكتسابه بالقسمة فلأن القسمة مستلزمة لأقسام غير معينة، فإن وضع قسم منها على التعيين ليكون حدا كان ذلك وضعا مفيدًا لا مستفادًا من القسمة. وإن استثني نقيض بعض الأقسام لإنتاج الباقي كما يقال حد الإنسان إما حيوان ناطق وإما حيوان غير ناطق ؛ والثاني ليس بحد فالحد هو الأول. أو يقال الإنسان إما حيوان وإما غير حيوان لكنه لا يكون غير حيوان فهو إذن حيوان؛ وبهذا الطريق يثبت أنه ناطق أو مائت. ثم يجمع جميع هذه المحمولات المساوية له ويقال أيضًا إنها قول مفصل دال على ماهية الإنسان. وكل قول مفصل [دال] على ماهية الشيء فهو حده فيقال لا يسلم / أن القسمة منحصرة فيما ذكرتم من الأقسام؛ ولا يسلم أن المقدمة [١١] الاستثنائية صادقة لا بد من إقامة الدليل على كل واحد منهما. وبتقدير التسليم لا تكون القسمة وحدها مفيدة للحد. ولأنه بيان للشيء بما هو أخفى منه فإن حد الشيء وأجزاؤه أبين بالنسبة إليه من الغير. ولأن القول المفصَّل الذي مرَّ ذکره حد الحد فتوسطه في القياس يكون مصادرة على المطلوب الأول؛ فإن من يعتقد أن الحد لا يعرف إلا بالبرهان فلا يسلم أن ذلك القول حد وإلا لا يسلم أن هذه المحمولات المساوية للإنسان قول مفصل دال على ماهيته. فإنه إذا علم ذلك علم أنها حد له دون هذا التوسط فإن معنى الشيء لا يكون بينا لشيء إلا وأن يكون بينا له؛ إذ ليس هو شيء غير معناه فعلم أنتوسط حد الأكبر لأنه أبين للأصغر، أو حد الأصغر لأن الأكبر أبين له لا يفيد المطلوب.
And as for the fact that it cannot be acquired by division, it is because division necessitates indeterminate parts. If a part is specifically posited to be a definition, that is a useful posit, not obtained from the division. And if the negation of some parts is excluded to produce the remainder, as in saying 'The definition of man is either rational animal or irrational animal; the second is not a definition, so the first is the definition.' Or it is said 'Man is either animal or non-animal; but he is not non-animal, so he is animal; and by this way it is established that he is rational or mortal.' Then all those predicates equal to him are collected and it is also said that they are a detailed statement indicating the essence of man. And every detailed statement [indicating] the essence of a thing is its definition. So it is said: It is not conceded that the division is limited to the mentioned parts; and it is not conceded that the [11] conditional premise is true; a proof must be provided for each of them. Even if conceded, division alone does not yield the definition. And because it is an explanation of the thing by what is more hidden than it, for the definition and its parts are more evident relative to the thing than the other. And because the detailed statement mentioned is the definition of definition, so using it as a middle term in the syllogism would be a begging of the original question. For whoever believes that definition is only known by demonstration does not concede that statement is a definition; otherwise, he does not concede that these predicates equal to man form a detailed statement indicating his essence. For if that is known, it is known that they are his definition without this mediation, because the meaning of a thing is not evident for something unless it is evident for it; since it is nothing other than its meaning. So it is known that using the definition of the major because it is more evident for the minor, or the definition of the minor because the major is more evident for it, does not yield the desired result.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that definition can be acquired through the method of division (qisma). The author gives several arguments. First, division yields indeterminate parts; picking one specifically as a definition is a prior imposition, not derived from the division. Second, even using exclusive disjunction, as in defining man by eliminating 'non-rational animal', the division may not be exhaustive or the conditional premise may be false; each needs proof. Third, even if conceded, division alone does not produce the definition; it relies on prior knowledge. Fourth, division explains a thing by what is more hidden (the parts are more evident than the whole, but using the detailed statement as a definition is circular because it assumes what a definition is). Fifth, the detailed statement is a definition of definition (a meta-definition), so using it in a syllogism begs the question: one who denies that definition is known only through demonstration will not accept that statement as a definition. Thus, the method of division fails to provide new knowledge of definitions.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Logical division, i.e., dividing a genus into species by differentiate.
الحد (al-hadd): Definition, i.e., a statement indicating the essence of a thing.
القول المفصل (al-qawl al-mufassal): A detailed statement comprising all essential predicates that indicate the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the division method for acquiring definitions?
A) Because division requires exhaustive enumeration, which is impossible.
B) Because division yields only indeterminate parts and begs the question.
C) Because division is only useful for rhetorical purposes.
D) Because division only works for accidents, not essences.

Key Takeaway
Division cannot generate new definitional knowledge; it merely presupposes the definition it claims to produce.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'sunnah' in usul al-fiqh. If someone attempts to define sunnah by dividing it into mu'akkadah and ghayr mu'akkadah, then selecting one as the definition, explain using the author's critique why this method fails to provide new knowledge of what sunnah is.

In a debate on the definition of 'ijma'', one party proposes to define it by dividing consensus into 'ijma' of the companions and 'ijma' of the mujtahids, then eliminating one part. Why Syllogism Cannot Define
Refutation of syllogism for definition acquisition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a categorical syllogism cannot produce a real definition (ḥadd) and cite the author's two arguments.

Core Idea
Syllogism predication does not equal definition; the middle term overreaches.

Many assume a syllogism can yield a definition of the minor term. This lesson unpacks the author's critique: the major term would need to be a definition of the minor, but the syllogism only shows predication, not definitional identity. Moreover, the middle term applies to the minor's differentiae and properties, so the major cannot be a definition of those either. Understanding this failure clarifies why definitions must come from a different logical method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that a categorical syllogism can be used to acquire a definition (ḥadd) of the minor term. Locate where the author states this is impossible.
2
Extract the first argument: syllogism yields predication, not definition
The syllogism only predicates the major (al-akbar) of the minor (al-aṣghar) in a certain way. But being predicated of something does not mean it is its definition. Look for the phrase 'لا يلزم من القياس'.
3
Extract the second argument: the middle term applies to differentiae
The middle term (al-awsaṭ) applies not only to the minor but also to its differentiae and properties. Therefore the major cannot be a definition of those, making the major premise false if it claims universality.
4
Formalize the objection in your own words
Write a brief summary: For a syllogism to produce a definition, the conclusion would need to state 'the minor is defined by the major'. But the syllogistic conclusion only states that the major is predicated of the minor, which is a weaker relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالماهية. ولأن الكبرى كاذبة حينئذ فإن الأوسط كما حصل للأصغر حصل أيضًا لفصوله وخواصه؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون الأكبر حدا لشيء منها. وأما الثالث فلأن القياس حينئذ لا ينتج إلا حمل الأكبر على الأصغر بالوجه الذي مر ذكره؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان. ولأن المطلوب في هذا المقام هو أن يكون الأكبر حدا للأصغر؛ وذلك لا يلزم من القياس. وأما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون اكتسابه بالقسمة فلأن القسمة مستلزمة لأقسام غير معينة، فإن وضع قسم منها على التعيين ليكون حدا كان ذلك وضعا مفيدًا لا مستفادًا من القسمة. وإن استثني نقيض بعض الأقسام لإنتاج الباقي كما يقال حد الإنسان إما حيوان ناطق وإما حيوان غير ناطق ؛ والثاني ليس بحد فالحد هو الأول. أو يقال الإنسان إما حيوان وإما غير حيوان لكنه لا يكون غير حيوان فهو إذن حيوان؛ وبهذا الطريق يثبت أنه ناطق أو مائت. ثم يجمع جميع هذه المحمولات المساوية له ويقال أيضًا إنها قول مفصل دال على ماهية الإنسان. وكل قول مفصل دال على ماهية الشيء فهو حده فيقال لا يسلم أن القسمة منحصرة فيما ذكرتم من الأقسام؛ ولا يسلم أن المقدمة الاستثنائية صادقة لا بد من إقامة الدليل على كل واحد منهما. وبتقدير التسليم لا تكون القسمة وحدها مفيدة للحد. ولأنه بيان للشيء بما هو أخفى منه فإن حد الشيء وأجزاؤه أبين بالنسبة إليه من الغير. ولأن القول المفصل الذي مر ذكره حد الحد فتوسطه في القياس يكون مصادرة على المطلوب الأول؛ فإن من يعتقد أن الحد لا يعرف إلا بالبرهان فلا يسلم أن ذلك القول حد وإلا لا يسلم أن هذه المحمولات المساوية للإنسان قول مفصل دال على ماهيته. فإنه إذا علم ذلك علم أنها حد له دون هذا التوسط فإن معنى الشيء لا يكون بينا لشيء إلا وأن يكون بينا له؛ إذ ليس هو شيء غير معناه فعلم أن توسط حد الأكبر لأنه أبين للأصغر، أو حد الأصغر لأن الأكبر أبين له لا يفيد المطلوب. وأما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون اكتسابه بالاستقراء فلأن الحد إذا حمل على الجزئيات بعد استقرائها على أنه حد لكل منها من حيث هو معين فباطل لما بينا أن الأشخاص من حيث هي أشخاص لا حدود لها لكونها من الفاسدات وإن سلمنا ذلك لكن حد الأشخاص الواقعة تحت النوع لا يكون حدا للنوع؛ فإن لكل واحد من الأشخاص ما ليس للنوع. وإذا حمل عليها على أنه حد نوعها فهو مصادرة على المطلوب. وإذا حمل عليها مطلقا فلا يلزم منه أن يكون حدا له وإن لزم منه حمله عليه لاحتمال أن يكون حمله عليه بطريق آخر غير حمل الحد على المحدود. وكما أنه لا يمكن اكتسابه بما مر فكذلك لا يمكن من حد الضد كما أن حده ضد ضد ضده ؛ فإن حد الضد لا يخلو من أن يكتسب من ضده وحينئذ يلزم الدور، أو من غير ضده؛ وذلك في حيز المنع إلا وأن يكون ذلك الغير هو حد هذا الشيء . ثم حد أحد الضدين ليس أعرف من حد الضد الآخر؛ فكيف يمكن اكتسابه ولأنه لا يلزم أن يكون لكل محددة ضد. وقد مر من قبل ما لا يكون قابلا للضد به كالزوجية والفردية والاستقامة والانحناء وغير ذلك. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن من حد الضد. فإنه إذا أمكن فإما أن يفتقر إليه في الجملة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون محدود ما ليس بمحدود، أو لا يفتقر إليه أصلا وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون مفتقرًا إليه إذا لم يمكن من غيره، وإنه محال بل الطريق في اكتساب المحدود وأقسامها هو التركيب. وذلك بأن يؤخذ عدة من الأشخاص التي لا تنقسم من جملة المحدود جنسا كان المحدود أو نوعا؛ ويطلب جميع محمولاتها إلى الأمر العام الذي لا أعم فوقه. وتلك المحمولات قد تكون من الذاتيات، وقد تكون من العرضيات: فتقرر الذاتيات من العرضيات بالقوانين التي مر ذكرها . ثم يوجد الذاتي الأعم الذي لا أعم فوقه. ثم الذي دونه من الذاتيات كذلك على الترتيب إلى أن ينتهي
with the quiddity. And because the major premise is then false, for the middle term as it is obtained for the minor is also obtained for its differentiae and properties; and it is impossible for the major to be a definition of any of them. As for the third, because the syllogism then only yields the predication of the major of the minor in the manner previously mentioned; and that is among things that need no explanation. And because the desired thing in this context is that the major be a definition of the minor; and that does not follow from the syllogism. As for that its acquisition cannot be by division, because division entails indefinite divisions, for positing one of the divisions determinately to be a definition would be a positing that is beneficial, not derived from division. And if one excludes the contradictory of some divisions to produce the remainder, as it is said: The definition of man is either rational animal or non-rational animal; the second is not a definition, so the definition is the first. Or it is said: Man is either animal or non-animal, but he is not non-animal, therefore he is animal; and by this way it is established that he is rational or mortal. Then all these predicates equivalent to him are collected, and it is also said: They are a detailed statement indicating the quiddity of man. And every detailed statement indicating the quiddity of a thing is its definition. Then it is said: It is not conceded that the division is restricted to the divisions you mentioned; nor is it conceded that the conditional premise is true; it is necessary to adduce a proof for each of them. And on the supposition of conceding, division alone is not beneficial for the definition. And because it is an exposition of a thing by what is more hidden than it, for the definition of a thing and its parts are more manifest relative to it than others. And because the detailed statement mentioned is the definition of definition, so its mediation in the syllogism is a petition of the first desired; for whoever believes that the definition is known only by demonstration does not concede that that statement is a definition, otherwise he does not concede that these predicates equivalent to man are a detailed statement indicating his quiddity. For if that were known, it would be known that they are a definition for him without this mediation, for the meaning of a thing is not manifest to a thing unless it is manifest to it; since it is nothing other than its meaning. So know that the mediation of the definition of the major because it is more manifest for the minor, or the definition of the minor because the major is more manifest for it, does not yield the desired. As for that its acquisition cannot be by induction, because if the definition is predicated of the particulars after inducting them as a definition of each of them insofar as it is determinate, it is false because we have shown that individuals insofar as they are individuals have no definitions because they are among corruptibles. And if we concede that, still the definition of individuals falling under a species is not a definition of the species; for each individual has what the species does not have. And if it is predicated of them as a definition of their species, it is a petition of the desired. And if it is predicated absolutely, it does not necessarily follow that it is a definition of it, even if its predication of it follows, because of the possibility that its predication of it is by another way other than the predication of the definition of the defined. And just as its acquisition cannot be by what has passed, so too it cannot be from the definition of the opposite, as that its definition is the opposite of its opposite; for the definition of the opposite is not free from being acquired from its opposite, in which case circularity follows, or from other than its opposite; and that is subject to denial unless that other is the definition of this thing. Moreover, the definition of one opposite is not more known than the definition of the other opposite; so how can it be acquired? And because it is not necessary that every defined has an opposite. There has already passed what is not receptive of an opposite, like evenness and oddness, straightness and curvature, and others. And even if it were so, it would not be possible from the definition of the opposite. For if it were possible, either it would be in need of it in general; in which case it would follow that what is undefined is defined; or it would not be in need of it at all, in which case it would follow that it is not in need of it when it cannot be from other than it, and that is impossible. Rather, the way in acquiring the defined and its divisions is composition. That is by taking a number of individuals that are not divisible from the totality of the defined, whether the defined is a genus or a species; and seeking all their predicates up to the universal term above which there is no more universal. And those predicates may be essential, and may be accidental: so the essential are distinguished from the accidental by the rules that have been mentioned. Then the most universal essential that has nothing more universal above it is found. Then what is below it among the essentials likewise in order until it ends.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of four alleged methods for acquiring definitions (ḥadd), followed by the proposal of the correct method, which is composition (tarkīb). The author first argues against acquiring definition through syllogism. The syllogism fails because the major term would need to be a definition of the minor, but the syllogism only predicates the major of the minor without guaranteeing that predication is definitional. Moreover, the middle term applies to the minor's differentiae and properties, making the major not a definition of them. The second refutation targets division (qisma). Division yields indefinite branches; to obtain a definition, one must arbitrarily select a branch, which is not derived from division itself. Even if one uses exclusive division (e.g., man is either rational or non-rational animal), this presumes the truth of the conditional premise and the exhaustive nature of the division, both of which require proof. Thus division alone does not produce the definition. The third refutation concerns induction (istiqrā'). Induction cannot yield a definition because individuals as such have no definitions (they are corruptible). Even if we concede definitions for individuals, the definition of individuals does not extend to the species (each individual has unique accidents). Predicating a definition across individuals absolutely does not guarantee it is a definition of the species. The fourth refutation addresses acquiring definition from the opposite (ḍidd). This leads to circularity (if the definition of one opposite depends on the other) or requires an external definition, which is essentially conceding the point. Moreover, not all things have opposites (e.g., evenness, straightness). Thus these four methods are invalid. The correct method is composition (tarkīb): one takes several indivisible individuals of the genus or species, collects all their predicates up to the most universal, separates essential (dhātiyyāt) from accidental (ʿaraḍiyyāt) using established rules, and then arranges the essential predicates from the most universal downward until the definition is reached. The text cuts off mid-explanation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the syllogism fail to produce a definition for the minor term?
A) Because the major premise is always false.
B) Because the syllogism only shows predication, not definitional identity, and the middle term applies to the minor's differentiae.
C) Because the minor term has no definition due to its corruptibility.
D) Because the syllogism requires a conditional premise that cannot be established.

Key Takeaway
Syllogistic predication does not entail definition; definition requires a stronger logical relation that syllogism cannot provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a syllogism that proves that nabīdh (date wine) is forbidden (ḥarām), then explain why this syllogism does not provide a definition of 'nabīdh'.
?
Identify the middle term in the following syllogism: 'Every intoxicant (musakkir) is forbidden; nabīdh is an intoxicant; therefore nabīdh is forbidden.' Explain why 'intoxicant' is not a definition of 'nabīdh'.
